Abstract
The purpose of this thesis was to validate the newly developed "The early warning method".
The early warning method can identify cascading outage scenarios in a power system,
using the graph theoretic, betweenness centrality approach, for initial fault selection. This
approach determines branches (i.e. lines or transformers) in a power system which, when
disconnected, may cause cascading outages based on their location in the power system.
Identifying such scenarios is valuable for the system operator, as it provides information
on the distance from the current operating state, to instability. The early warning method
had previously only been validated on the medium sized Nordic32 test system, and in this
thesis a validation is therefore performed on a large-scale power system.
The large-scale power system is a detailed model of the Swedish transmission grid with
interconnections to simpliﬁed models of Norway, Denmark, and Finland. This model
was created for an earlier project, and the thesis therefore contains descriptions of the
modiﬁcations and extensions which have been made in order to make the model applicable
for this thesis.
The early warning method can use either the well-known Newton-Raphson or the newly
developed TESCA method to perform static security assessments of the system. The thesis
contains an N-1 and N-2 assessment using both methods.
It was investigated if it was possible to manually identify a cascading outage scenario,
which the early warning method did not identify. This was found possible, and the cause of
this was concluded to be the use of betweenness centrality for the identiﬁcation of branches
to investigate.
The thesis concludes that the early warning method is very reliable for the N-1 assessment,
while in the N-2 assessment, the method has a signiﬁcant decrease in the number of correct
cascading outage predictions. The TESCA method was found to be more accurate than
the Newton-Raphson method in both assessments, however, this method was also found
to be more computationally heavy. The cause of this decrease in correct predictions was
investigated, and it was found that the Newton-Raphson method’s way of balancing the
power ﬂow, and the load modeling in both the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method
may be problematic.

Introduction 1
1.1
Background
A constant supply of electric power is essential in today’s modern society. Many applications
require a constant supply of electricity to function. Such applications may range from city
traﬃc lights, railway networks, heating/cooling systems in e.g. warehouses or domestic
homes, to banks or hospitals. Even though short-term power storage devices exist, these
are neither installed, nor capable of fully covering a large-scale blackout. It is therefore
of major importance for system operators to avoid blackouts, and this ﬁeld is therefore
subject to ongoing research.
Maintaining a stable operation of a power system is challenging. Modern day power systems
are large, complex, and consist of numerous types of components. Such components may
be generators, transformers, transmission lines, and reactive power compensators, just
to mention some. To further increase complexity, the power systems in many countries
are interconnected with their neighboring countries, e.g. Denmark is interconnected with
Sweden, Norway, and Germany. Such interconnections add economic beneﬁts, but does
also introduce the risk of cross-regional blackouts. The previous years have shown that
major blackouts can occur. An example of this is the blackout of southern Sweden and
eastern Denmark which occurred on September 23, 2003. This blackout aﬀected ﬁve million
people and lasted for approximately 6.5 hours [1, p. 4].
Power system components may fail for various reasons. A generator may fail due to
excessive excitation current, over/under voltage or frequency. A transformer may fail due
to insulation failure and transmission lines may fail because of a lightning strike, line
overloading, or untrimmed trees causing short circuits. A complex power system with
many components thereby has an immense amount of possible fault sources.
Component failures in power systems are often associated. Overloading of one transmission
line may be caused by the tripping of another transmission line. The trip of a large
generator may cause a severe dip in the system frequency causing another generator to
trip. This phenomenon is known as cascading failures/outages. Cascading outages are
often included in major blackouts, which was also the case in the 2003 Swedish/Danish
blackout. Another more severe example of a blackout caused by cascading outages is the
Northeast blackout of 2003, which aﬀected the eastern U.S. and Canada. This blackout
aﬀected an estimated 50 million people, and lasted for up to four days in some parts of
the aﬀected area. It took approximately seven minutes from the initiating event, until the
ﬁnal blackout state had been reached [2, p 1].
1
==PAGE:14==
1. Introduction
Identifying fault scenarios where cascading outages can occur is therefore of great interest to
the system operators, so that these can be avoided. However, performing an identiﬁcation
of such scenarios is not a simple task. The number of fault scenarios leading to cascading
outages in a given power system increases drastically as the number of system components
increases. Whether cascading outages occur or not for a given fault depends on the power
ﬂow in the system, which changes continuously. It is therefore not realistic to calculate the
outcome of all possible fault scenarios due to the amount of these.
Various methods for analyzing only a selection of fault scenarios in a power system towards
cascading outages have been developed. One of these methods will be applied in this study
on a model of the Swedish transmission grid.
1.2
Thesis Objective
The objective of this thesis is to apply, test and validate a newly developed method for
analyzing cascading outages of transmission lines in a power system. The concerned power
system is modeled in the PSS/E V.33 software, and contains a detailed model of the
Swedish transmission grid along with interconnections to simpliﬁed models of Norway,
Denmark, and Finland.
As mentioned, it is not realistic to investigate all possible fault scenarios in a given power
system due to the large number of these. It is therefore necessary to select a set of the most
critical scenarios. In this thesis, the selection is performed, using a novel method developed
by Pauli Fríðheim Petersen, hereafter referred to as the early warning method. Based on
a user speciﬁed search depth and width, the early warning method calculates scenarios
where the disconnection of one or more branches (i.e. transmission lines or transformers)
lead to cascading outages of transmission lines. The output of the early warning method is
thus a set of scenarios which are assessed to cause cascading outages.
Due to the recent development of the early warning method, it has only been tested on
the medium-size Nordic32 test system. It is therefore of interest to validate the method on
a large-scale system - this validation is performed in this thesis.
The thesis will contain the following subjects:
• Modiﬁcation and extension of a pre-existing PSS/E model, so it is applicable for this
study.
• Application of the early warning method on the modiﬁed PSS/E model to perform
an N-1 and N-2 assessment. Then an attempt at validating the identiﬁed scenarios
will be made through time domain simulations performed in the PSS/E model.
• An investigation will be carried out to ascertain if the early warning method identiﬁes
an N-1 and N-2 scenario, which is known in advance to cause cascading outages in
the PSS/E model. This is another way of validating the early warning method.
• The early warning method is designed to use either the Newton-Raphson method
or the newly developed TESCA method to determine the stability of the system
following the disconnection of a branch. This thesis will investigate the behavior of
the early warning method using both methods.
• A detailed analysis of a scenario where cascading outages lead to a system blackout.
2
==PAGE:15==
1.3. Learning Objectives
1.3
Learning Objectives
After having completed this thesis, the student will be able to:
1. Identify the PSS/E model requirements for a dynamic stability study of this type.
2. Gain an understanding of overcurrent relays and the modeling of these.
3. Modify and extend an already existing PSS/E simulation model to this study.
4. Apply a state of the art research method for analyzing cascading outages in electric
power systems.
5. Apply PSS/E to perform an extensive power system simulation study.
6. Analyze and simulate the mechanisms causing blackouts through cascading outages.
1.4
Thesis Structure
The following is a list of the chapters in the thesis, along with a description of the contents.
Chapter 2, Theory: contains deﬁnitions and describes the theory used throughout the
thesis. This includes a deﬁnition of the terms "Cascading outage" and "N-k security". A
brief description of topics within the ﬁeld of power system stability, especially transient
stability, as this is very relevant for the study of cascading outages. A section on power ﬂow
analysis where the workings of the Newton-Raphson and TESCA methods are introduced.
The reason for the need for line overcurrent protection relays is given, and lastly the
chapter contains a description of load modeling in PSS/E.
Chapter 3, The Early Warning Method: contains an overall description of the early
warning method. To understand the workings of the method, a ﬂowchart of this is provided.
The graphical user interface designed for the early warning method is also shown, along
with an example of how to interpret the results shown in this. Finally, the concept of
betweenness centrality, which is an essential part of the early warning method, is described.
Chapter 4, The Simulation Model: contains a description of the developed model,
the performed modiﬁcations and extensions. This includes the addition of line overcurrent
relays and lines connected to transformers, dividing single-busbar substations with two
voltage levels into two busbars, and modiﬁcation of transformer data. This chapter also
contains the line loadings and bus voltages based on the initial load ﬂow analysis.
Chapter 5, Test and Validation of the Early Warning Method: begins with a
description of how the simulations are performed in PSS/E, along with a description of
how the stability is assessed following line trips. An N-1 and N-2 cascading outage scenario,
which is known in advance, is presented and described in detail. Then the inﬂuence of
search width on the computation time and identiﬁed scenarios is investigated, which leads
to a chosen search width. This search width is used for two N-2 assessments, using the
Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method. These assessments, along with a N-1 assessment
are then analyzed compared with time domain simulations.
Chapter 6, Conclusion: contains a discussion of the observations made throughout
the thesis regarding the early warning method, the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA
methods. A set of suggestions for future work is presented, and the chapter ﬁnishes with
an evaluation of the learning objectives presented in the introduction.
3
==PAGE:16==
Theory 2
This chapter describes the terms and theory used throughout the thesis. The contents in
this chapter will serve as reference in the following analysis.
2.1
Deﬁnition of Cascading Outage
This thesis investigates the phenomena of cascading outages of transmission lines in a power
system, and it is therefore necessary to have a formal deﬁnition of this term. According to
an article written by the "IEEE Task Force on Understanding, Predection, Mitigation, and
Restoration of Cascading Failures of the IEEE PES Computing and Analytical Methods
Subcomitee (CAMS)" [3, p. 631], the following deﬁnition applies:
"A cascading outage is a sequence of events in which an initial disturbance, or
set of disturbances, triggers a sequence of one or more dependent component
outages" [3, p. 631].
From this deﬁnition, it is clear, that a cascading outage may be just a single system
component that trips due to an initial disturbance. This could for instance be a scenario
where one of two parallel lines trip, causing the other line to trip. Even if no more events
take place in the system, this is classiﬁed as a cascading outage of the second line according
to this deﬁnition. Cascading outages may, however, also be more extensive and aﬀect
more components. This could for instance be a scenario where the tripping of the two
parallel lines creates a sequence of failures in the system, which eventually leads to a
system blackout.
2.2
Deﬁnition of N-k Security
Within the analysis of power system stability, the term N-k security is commonly used. This
term states that for a power system with N components that can possibly fail (generators,
transformers, transmission lines etc.), the system is N-k secure if it can remain stable
following the loss of any k components. From time to time, power system components
will unavoidably be aﬀected by faults and failures such as lightning strikes on overhead
lines, mechanical failures in power plants, or ﬁres in substations/transformers. Therefore,
power systems are in general designed and operated in a way so that the system can
withstand the loss of any one component, and it is thereby N-1 secure. Ensuring e.g. N-2
security is more demanding. It requires that there is enough reserve generation capacity
to compensate for the loss of any two generators and enough transmission capacity to
handle the changed power ﬂow following the outage of any two lines - or combinations with
4
==PAGE:17==
2.3. Power System Stability
other components. Therefore, even though a scenario where two or more consecutive faults
occur is theoretically possible, power systems are in general not designed/operated to be
secure for k>1. There will always be a sequence of events which can lead a power system
to a blackout, and power systems are therefore only designed to be able to withstand all
credible contingencies.
2.3
Power System Stability
The investigation performed this thesis falls within the subject of power system stability.
Theory within this subject is therefore deﬁned in this chapter, as this is needed in the
following analysis to validate the scenarios identiﬁed by the early warning method.
According to Prabha Kundur, the term power system stability is deﬁned as follows:
"Power system stability may be broadly deﬁned as that property of a power
system that enables it to remain in a state of operating equilibrium under
normal operating conditions and to regain an acceptable state of equilibrium
after being subjected to a disturbance" [4, p. 17].
The early warning method analyzes the stability of a power system following one or more
branch trips. These branch trips can be seen as the disturbance Kundur mentions in the
power system stability deﬁnition. Power system stability is essentially a single problem.
However, due to the large complexity of this problem, it is convenient to classify power
system stability into appropriate categories. These categories are commonly deﬁned as
shown in Figure 2.1.
Power System
Stability
Frequency
Stability
Voltage Stability
Rotor Angle
Stability
Small Signal
Stability
Transient Stability
Small Disturbance
Large Disturbance
Figure 2.1: Classiﬁcation of power system stability.
The three overall categories which are: rotor angle stability, frequency stability and voltage
stability will now be deﬁned. These types of instability can all be present following the
branch trip disturbances applied to the system in the upcoming analysis.
5
==PAGE:18==
2. Theory
2.3.1
Rotor Angle Stability
Rotor angle stability is concerned with the behavior, and ability of the synchronous
machines in a power system to remain in synchronism following a disturbance. Under
steady-state operation, all synchronous machines will be operating at a constant speed
and hence have constant rotor angles. The magnitudes of these angles depend on the
load and location of the synchronous machines in the system. The constant rotor angle
in steady-state is caused by the fact that there is a state of equilibrium between the
mechanical input torque/power on the shaft of the machine and the electrical counter
torque/power caused by the power system to which the machine is connected. Following
the occurrence of a disturbance, this equilibrium will be disturbed, and the rotor will either
accelerate of decelerate, which obviously changes the rotor angle. Such a disturbance can
mathematically be described as shown in Eq. (2.1). This equation shows that a mismatch
between the driving mechanical torque Tm and the developed electromagnetic torque Te,
creates an accelerating torque:
Ta = Tm −Te
(2.1)
For a generator which has a rotor angular displacement with respect to the stationary axis
on the stator θm, and where the parameter J represents the combined moment of inertia
on the prime mover and generator (neglecting frictional and damping torques), Eq. (2.1)
can be extended as:
J d2θm
dt2
= Ta = Tm −Te
(2.2)
The ﬁrst derivative of the angular displacement is the angular velocity, and the second
derivative, which is present in Eq. (2.2), is the angular acceleration of the rotor. This
equation thereby shows how an angular acceleration can be created, when there is a
mismatch between the mechanical and electromagnetic torque, Tm ̸= Te. It is shown in
Eq. (2.2) how this angular acceleration is directly proportional with the combined moment
of inertia of the generator and turbine, J. This parameter has the unit kg · m2. This unit
may be inconvenient to work with, and J is therefore often normalized in the shape of the
inertia constant H. This inertia constant is deﬁned by:
H = 1
2
J2
ω0m
Sbase
(2.3)
where ω0m is the rated angular velocity in mechanical radians per second, and Sbase is the
base power for the concerned synchronous machine. Another way of describing the inertia
constant is by the following equation:
H = stored kinetic energy in MJ at rated speed
machine rating in MVA
(2.4)
The value of H ranges from 1 to 10 seconds depending on the size and type of
machine [5, p. 463]. The inertia of the dynamic generator models are deﬁned in PSS/E
using this inertia constant.
Through various rewritings of Eq. (2.2) and Eq. (2.3), the following equation, commonly
referred to as the swing equation, is obtained from which it should be noted that the powers
6
==PAGE:19==
2.3. Power System Stability
and torques are given in per-unit:
2H
ω0
d2δ
dt2 = Pm −Pe = Pm −Pmax sin δ
(2.5)
From the swing equation, a number of conclusions can be made. Firstly, the acceleration
in rotor angle δ is given by the mismatch between the mechanical power input and
electromagnetic power output, which is identical to the mismatch between input and
output torque due to the per-unit system. Based on the sign of these two terms, the rotor
angle acceleration can be either positive or negative, depending on the type of disturbance
which the machine is exposed to. It is also seen that there is a maximum electrical power
which the synchronous machine can produce, given by Pmax. This level of electrical output
power is proportional to sin δ, which obviously makes the electrical output power as a
function of the rotor angle highly nonlinear. Finally, the swing equation can be rewritten
to show that the acceleration for a given mismatch between electrical and mechanical
power is proportional with the synchronous speed ω0.
The analysis of rotor angle stability is divided into two subcategories, which allows the
use of diﬀerent analysis techniques. The two categories are: small signal stability, and
transient stability.
2.3.1.1
Small-Signal Stability
Small-signal stability is concerned with the ability of a synchronous machines in a power
system to remain in synchronism when subjected to small disturbances.
Such small
disturbances may be the constant variation in load and generation. Disturbances are
considered to be small if it is permissible to linearize system equations for the analysis
around an initial operating point. The branch disconnection disturbances applied in this
study do not fall within this category as these have a larger impact on the rotor angles, and
the linearization approach is therefore not suﬃcient. Consequently, small-signal stability
will not be treated any further in this thesis.
2.3.1.2
Transient Stability
Transient stability deals with the eﬀect of large disturbances to a power system. Such
disturbances may be the occurrence of a fault, outage of a line, or loss of load/generation.
Since this study investigates the behavior of a power system during cascading outages, the
topic of transient stability is highly relevant. The response of the system following such
a disturbance is large excursions of generator rotor angles, changes in power ﬂows, bus
voltages and other system variables. According to [6, p. 1390] the time frame of interest in
transient stability studies is usually 3 to 5 seconds following the disturbance. This may be
extended to 10 to 20 seconds for very large systems.
A topic commonly referred to within transient stability is the equal-area criterion. Using
this criterion is a way of determining if a synchronous machine loses synchronism, following
a given disturbance. The equal-area criterion is only applicable to a single-machine system
7
==PAGE:20==
2. Theory
connected to an inﬁnite bus. The equal-area criterion is, however, useful, as it gives insight
into the physical behavior of a machine following a disturbance. In relation to this study,
the TESCA method, which is described in Section 2.4.5, is capable of creating such a
single-machine system where the equal-area criterion can be applied.
A single-machine system is shown in Figure 2.2. Here, a synchronous generator with
an internal voltage source denoted E′∠δ and transient reactance X′
d, is connected to an
inﬁnite busbar denoted EB∠0 through two parallel lossless transmission lines, each with a
series reactance of XL. If one of the two lines connecting the generator to the inﬁnite bus
should trip, the total system reactance connected to the generator and inﬁnite bus would
double, assuming identical lines.
X′
d

Figure 2.2: Equivalent circuit of a synchronous generator represented by the classical model,
connected to an inﬁnite bus through two identical transmission lines.
In a steady-state situation, the mechanical input power equals the electrical output power
and there is no acceleration. Equation (2.5) can hereby be reduced to:
Pe = Pmax sin δ =

sin δ
(2.6)
From Eq. (2.6) it is clear that the maximum power which can be transferred has a strong
dependence on the reactance between the generator internal voltage source and the inﬁnite
busbar. This is clearly shown when drawing a power-angle curve for the two scenarios with
one and two lines in service. This curve is shown in Figure 2.3.
8
==PAGE:21==

Figure 2.3: Power-angle relationship (P −δ curve) for the system shown in Figure 2.2.
In the event of a disturbance such as a line trip, a certain time must pass before the
generator reaches a new state of equilibrium or loses synchronism. The way by which a
new equilibrium is obtained will now be described in steps based on Figure 2.3.
1. The system is initially operating in a steady-state with the mechanical power input
Pm equal to the electrical power output Pe. For the system with both lines in service,
this results in an angle of δa, and the operating point of the generator is thereby
shown in point a.
2. Once the line trips, the total reactance between the generator’s internal voltage
source and the inﬁnite bus increases. The maximum power has decreased according to
Eq. (2.6), and is now given by the orange power-angle relationship curve. The rotor
angle should therefore change to δc, as this is the angle where Pm = Pe. However,
due to the inertia of the rotor, the rotor angle cannot change instantaneously. The
reactance does, however, change instantaneously when the line trips. This causes the
electrical power output to decrease to Pb, while maintaining the same angle δa. The
generator is now at point b.
3. When the generator is at point b, there is an excess of mechanical input power
over the electrical output power, Pm > Pe. This causes an acceleration of the rotor,
towards the point where Pm = Pe which stores kinetic energy in the process and
increases the rotor angle.
4. When the rotor angle reaches point c, Pm = Pe, and according to Eq. (2.5) the
acceleration is zero. There is, however, a given amount of kinetic energy stored in
the rotor at this point. This causes the rotor angle to keep increasing above δc.
5. When the generator reaches angles larger than δc, there is an excess of electrical
energy over the mechanical energy. This causes the rotor to decelerate, and the angle
decreases. The rotor decelerates until a peak angle, which in this case is δd. At this
point, the rotor has used the kinetic energy which was gained during the increase of
angle from δa to δc, and the rotor angle begins to decrease.
6. The rotor will hereafter oscillate back and forth around the steady-state angle δc,
where Pm = Pe, until point c is reached, without any excess energy stored in the
9
==PAGE:22==
2. Theory
rotor. It is the damping in the machine which causes the oscillations in rotor angle
to decrease. This damping is, among others, caused by the damping (armortisseur)
windings in the rotor. It should be noted that damping is neglected in Figure 2.2.
These steps are for a case where the generator maintains synchronism following the trip
of a line. It is, however, not always the case that synchronism is maintained following a
disturbance. For a generator to lose synchronism following a line trip, the kinetic energy
stored in the rotor during the period where Pm > Pe must be greater than the energy
which can decelerate the rotor in the period where Pe > Pm. By denoting these two
energies as the areas A1 and A2 in Figure 2.3, the equal-area criterion states that A1 = A2
for the generator to maintain synchronism following a disturbance. If A1 > A2, then the
generator is not able to decrease its rotor angle following the disturbance, and it will lose
synchronism.
Based on the power-angle curve in Figure 2.3 and the described steps for the transition of
generator rotor angle, a set of factors aﬀecting the transient stability of a generator can be
deﬁned. These are:
• The loading of the generator. The higher the loading, the higher initial rotor angle,
and thereby smaller room for the area A2 where excess kinetic energy should be
dissipated.
• The postfault system reactance. Since the maximum transferable power is inversely
proportional to the total reactance seen from the generator internal voltage source,
to the inﬁnite bus, this reactance has a large impact on the transient stability.
• The generator reactance. This reactance is added to the total reactance in the system,
and a smaller reactance thereby results in larger maximum power transfer capability.
• The generator inertia. A larger inertia usually means a physically heavier rotor.
When the rotor increases in weight, it is not capable of accelerating as fast as if it
was lighter. So for larger inertia H in Eq. (2.5), the acceleration d2δ/dt2 is smaller,
for a given mismatch between Pe and Pm.
• Magnitude of the generator’s internal voltage source. The maximum electrical power
is directly proportional to the magnitude of the internal voltage source E′. Increasing
this magnitude thereby increases the maximum power, and hence reduces the rotor
angle needed for a given power transfer. The magnitude of this voltage source can
be controlled by the ﬁeld excitation of the generator’s rotor.
• Magnitude of the inﬁnite bus voltage. Using the same argument as for the generator’s
internal voltage source, increasing the bus voltage magnitude decreases the needed
rotor angle for a given amount of power transfer.
Relating this theory to the cascading outages of transmission lines investigated in this
study, it is concluded that a key factor inﬂuencing the transient stability in a system is the
sudden change in the system reactance, which is caused by the line trips. Every time a
line trips the system reactance seen from each generator changes, and if the disturbance is
suﬃciently severe, a generator can lose synchronism. Figure 2.3 shows a scenario where
the generator maintains synchronism because A1 = A2. If, however, the system reactance
following the line trip had been larger, this might not have been the case. It will be
shown in time domain simulations included later in the thesis that loss of synchronism is a
common culprit causing instability when cascading outages of lines occur.
10
==PAGE:23==
2.3. Power System Stability
2.3.2
Frequency Stability
Frequency stability refers to the ability of a power system to maintain a steady frequency
following a disturbance. The frequency in a system reﬂects the balance between the power
generation and consumption, which includes the losses in the system. During frequency
excursions, the voltage magnitudes in the system may change signiﬁcantly, which adds to
the load-generation imbalance. If the frequency is steady, then the total power generated is
equal to the total power consumed, and the system is in a state of equilibrium. Since the
demand in a power system constantly varies, it is necessary to have control systems which
can adapt the production of the generators to meet this varying demand, and thereby
maintain a steady frequency. This control action is performed by the governors on the
generators which control the mechanical input power to each generator. The control may
be performed by e.g. changing the position on a steam valve in a thermal power plant, or
the water ﬂow in a hydro power plant.
When a disturbance occurs in a system, such as the trip of a transmission line, the
generation/consumption balance in the system changes. This causes a frequency excursion
of a given magnitude, depending on the severity and type of disturbance. The gradient of
the frequency excursion depends strongly on the total inertia in the system. This total
inertia can be calculated by Eq. (2.7), which equals the sum of all the rotational energy in
a system that consists of N synchronous machines, each with an inertia constant Hi and
power rating of Sbase,i.
Erot =
N
X
i
HiSbase,i
(2.7)
By knowing the total rotational energy in a system, the initial frequency gradient following
a disturbance of ∆P can be calculated by the following equation. It should be noted
that this equation is only valid at the time of the disturbance. Immediately after this,
the governors in the system will begin to aﬀect the generators in order to reach a new
steady-state frequency in the system, which changes the frequency gradient.
df

= ∆Pf0
2Erot
(2.8)
To illustrate the eﬀect of the inertia in the system when subjected to disturbances,
Figure 2.4 has been created. This ﬁgure shows how the frequency behaves following the
same disturbance with three diﬀerent levels of inertia. Here it is clearly seen how the green
curve, which represents the system with the largest inertia, experiences a smaller frequency
excursion than the blue curve, which represents the system with the smallest amount of
inertia. It is also seen how the initial frequency gradient is smallest for the green curve,
which corresponds with Eq. (2.8) because Erot is largest in this case.

When the frequency has reached a new steady level following a disturbance, it may be that
this frequency deviates from the nominal frequency. This is also shown in Figure 2.4. In
the post-disturbance case of under-frequency, secondary control must be activated that
frees generation reserves, so that the frequency can return to nominal. Such secondary
control is not implemented in the PSS/E model used for this thesis, and steady-state
frequency deviations will therefore occur in the time domain simulations following the line
trip disturbances.
2.3.3
Voltage Stability
Voltage stability refers to the ability of a power system to maintain steady voltages at all
buses in the system after being subjected to a disturbance from a given initial operating
condition [6, p. 1390]. A system becomes voltage unstable when a disturbance occurs
which causes a progressive and uncontrollable decrease in the voltage magnitudes in the
system. Just as frequency and active power are closely related, so are voltage and reactive
power. Reactive power compensation equipment is therefore used to control voltage in
a system. Such equipment may be the generators, which have a certain reactive power
import/export capability or shunt reactors/capacitors. According to [4, p. 27], the main
factor causing instability is the inability of the power system to meet the demand for
reactive power.
In addition to the strong relationship between reactive power and voltage, active power
and voltage are also related. A way of seeing this is by inspecting a PV-curve. Such a
curve can be created for a simple two-bus system consisting of an ideal voltage source in
series with a line reactance connected to a load, as shown in Figure 2.5.
12
==PAGE:25==

created. These PV-curves, also called nose curves, show the receiving end voltage, as a
function of the active power received.

Figure 2.6: PV curves of a two-bus power system with diﬀerent load power factors.
From the PV-curve in Figure 2.6, a number of conclusions can be made. Firstly, for lagging
power factors the voltage at the load bus immediately begins to decrease as the active
power transfer increases. For leading power factors this is also eventually the case, however,
there is an increase in voltage in between. It is also seen that there is a maximum power
which can be transferred for any given power factor. This maximum active power transfer
occurs when the voltage drop across the line reactance equals the voltage drop across the
load. After this point, any further reduction in load impedance would also reduce the
power consumption. Trying to transfer more active power is simply not physically possible.
If this is attempted in a power ﬂow study where the Newton-Raphson method is applied,
it will result in divergence.
13
==PAGE:26==
2. Theory
2.4
Power Flow Analysis
In relation with power system analysis, one of the most fundamental problems is the power
ﬂow problem, also known as load ﬂow analysis. This problem is complex to solve because
the loads in the system are constantly changing, and the system operator has no control
of this - it is completely controlled by the consumers. Furthermore, the inevitable losses
in the system are not increasing linearly as a function of the power ﬂow through e.g. the
transmission lines.
The basic idea of the power ﬂow analysis is to determine the complex voltages at all buses,
and the ﬂow of complex power to and from all buses in the system. Each bus thereby has
four associated variables: voltage magnitude, voltage angle, active power injection, and
reactive power injection. For every bus in the system, it is necessary to specify two of
these four variables, after which the remaining two variables will be calculated by means
of the load ﬂow algorithm. To set up the load ﬂow, it is necessary to have information
on the topology of the system, as well as values of line impedances, shunts, transformers,
loads and generators. Before any calculations can be performed, it is necessary to collect
the system data in an admittance matrix, which is used to set up the system equations.
2.4.1
The Admittance Matrix
Any given busbar which has generators, loads, or a combination of the two connected,
can be aggregated in a single component. This component represents the complex power
injection into the busbar. A busbar with a positive power injection supplies power to
the system, while a busbar with a negative injection draws power from the system. An
example of such a busbar with two lines connected is in shown in Figure 2.7. In this
ﬁgure, Pk and Qk are the active and reactive power injections at bus k, respectively.
Subscript G represents generation, and subscript L represents load at the busbar. The
total complex power injection to the busbar is thereby calculated from both the connected
power generation source and the load.
To bus j
To bus i
Pk = PG,k −PL,k
Qk = QG,k −QL,k
Sk = ⃗Vk ⃗Ik
∗= Pk + jQk
⃗Vk = Vk∠δk
Figure 2.7: A busbar k, with a complex power injection of Sk, connected to two busbars, j and i,
through two transmission lines.

circuit, which consists of one series admittance and two shunt admittances. If the two lines
in Figure 2.7 were represented in such a way, the current injection to the busbar would,

From Eq. (2.10) the following can be concluded:
• For a system with n busbars, the admittance matrix has dimensions n × n, and the
admittance matrix is therefore always symmetric.
• Since the series admittance from busbar i to busbar k is equal to the series admittance
from busbar k to busbar i, it can be generalized that Yki = Yik.
• The matrix is symmetric only if there are no phase-shifting transformers [4, p. 259].
• The elements of the admittance matrix will be nonzero only when there is a physical
connection between two busbars. Since there is a limited amount of connections
between the busbars in a large power system, the admittance matrix will be sparse.
• The diagonal elements called self admittances, Yii, are given as the algebraic sum of
all elements connected to busbar i.
• The oﬀ-diagonal elements called mutual admittances, Yij, are given as the negative
algebraic sum of all admittances connecting busbar i and j.
2.4.2

The system admittance matrix has now been described, and the power ﬂow equations
which is used in the Newton-Raphson method can be constructed based on this matrix.
These power ﬂow equations are two equations which express the active and reactive power
injection at a given busbar. These equations are functions of the complex voltages in the
system and the admittance matrix.

sum of the currents ﬂowing in the lines connected to that busbar. This is written as:

Equation (2.15) and Eq. (2.16) are the ﬁnal power ﬂow equations, which are algebraic and

2.4.3
Classiﬁcation of Busbars
The ﬁnal information which needs to be determined for the system before the power ﬂow
can be calculated is to select types for all the busbars. A busbar can be classiﬁed into
three categories [4, p. 255]:
• PV bus: Here, the active power injection and bus voltage magnitude is speciﬁed.
An example of such a busbar could be a generator set to produce a given amount
of active power and maintain a given voltage. The generator will then adjust its
reactive power import/export, to maintain this voltage. Therefore, Pi and Vi are
known, and Qi and θi are unknown.
• PQ bus: Here, the active and reactive injection power is speciﬁed. An example of
such a bus could be where a load is connected, which draws a constant amount of
power. Therefore, Pi and Qi are known, and Vi and θi are unknown.
• Slack (sometimes referred to as swing or inﬁnite bus) bus: This type of bus has the
voltage magnitude and angle speciﬁed, e.g. one per-unit with an angle of zero degrees.
Using an angle of zero degrees for the slack bus makes it a convenient reference bus
for the voltage angles in the system. The variables for the slack bus is then the
active and reactive power injection. It is necessary to have a slack bus in the system,
since it supplies whatever power is missing to keep the system in balance. This is for
instance the power loss, which depends on the power ﬂow, and hence is unknown
before the power ﬂow calculation. There can only be one slack bus in a system.
16
==PAGE:29==
2.4. Power Flow Analysis
2.4.4
The Newton-Raphson Method
In the previous sections it was described how the topology of a power system could be
described using an admittance matrix. The three bus-types (Section 2.4.3) and power ﬂow
equations (Eq. (2.15) and Eq. (2.16)) were also explained and derived. To solve the power
ﬂow for a given system, a set of power ﬂow equations is needed. The number and type (Pk
or Qk) of these power ﬂow equations is determined by the known power injections and
hence the bus types. At a PQ bus, both the active and reactive power injection is known,
and both power ﬂow equations given in (2.15) and Eq. (2.16) can therefore be written.
For a PV bus, it is only the active power injection which is known, and the power ﬂow
equation for the reactive power injection can therefore not be written for such a bus type.
For a slack bus none of the power ﬂow equations can be written. Assuming that bus 1 is
the slack bus and all other buses are PQ, the power ﬂow equations can be written in a
vector as:

The Newton-Raphson method uses Taylor series expansion to expand the power ﬂow
equations into a series with a number of derivative terms. For this type of analysis, it is,

estimate is close to the true solution [4, p. 261]. These ﬁrst derivative terms are gathered
in a matrix known as the Jacobian matrix. This matrix is given by:

Before the iterative process can begin, it is necessary to specify an initial guess, x = x0. A
reasonable guess is the ﬂat start where all voltage magnitudes are selected to one per-unit,
and all voltage angles are selected at zero degrees.
2.4.5
The TESCA Method
The early warning method assesses the stability of a system, using either the previously
described Newton-Raphson method or the newly developed Thévenin Equivalent based
Static Contingency Assessment method (TESCA). The TESCA method has been developed
by Jakob Glarbo Møller for a PhD project, completed at the Technical University of
Denmark, in September 2016.
As the name Thévenin Equivalent based Static Contingency Assessment indicates, this
method applies the concept of Thévenin equivalents for computing the steady-state power
ﬂow in a system, and hence assesses the stability. The TESCA method calculates a
Thévenin equivalent seen from each bus in a given system, which is used to investigate the
stability of the system. The stability is assessed in two diﬀerent ways, depending on the
investigated bus being a PV (generator) or PQ (load) bus. Bus types were described in
Section 2.4.3.
In the case of a PV bus, it is investigated if the connected generator is aperiodic unstable.
This investigation performed by ﬁrst calculating the total Thevénin impedance of the
remaining system, seen from the concerned PV bus. Knowing this impedance, and the
mechanical input power on the concerned generator, it can be investigated if there is a
point on the P −δ curve where the generator can operate (intersection between Pe(δ)
and Pm). An example of such a Thévenin equivalent circuit connected to a generator’s
PV bus, with a corresponding P −δ curve is shown in Figure 2.8. Initially, the Thévenin
impedance seen from the generator creates the blue curve where the generator operates at
angle δj. The generator is thereby stable. In the case of the loss of a line in the system,
the total Thévenin impedance seen from the PV bus will increase, causing the maximum
electrical output power to decrease, as shown in Eq. (2.6). A new state of the same system
where a line has been tripped is shown by the orange curve in Figure 2.8. In this case the
Thévenin impedance has increased so much that there is no longer an intersection between
the mechanical input power and the electrical output power. The generator is thereby
aperiodically unstable.
18
==PAGE:31==
2.4. Power Flow Analysis
Zth,j
PV
|Vj|∠δj
|Eth,j|∠0
Stability
assessment
δ
P
Pm
δj
Figure 2.8: TESCA stability assessment of a generator connected to a PV bus using a P −δ
curve.
In the case of a PQ bus, the TESCA method investigates if the bus is voltage stable.
Simply stated, voltage instability results from the attempt to operate beyond maximum
load power [7, p. 209]. To investigate this type of stability, a model of a load connected to
the Thévenin equivalent of the system must therefore be constructed. Such a circuit is
shown in Figure 2.9 where the load is represented by a voltage dependent current source.
This way of load modeling is chosen to represent the load as a constant power. Where a
P −δ curve was used to assess the stability at a PV bus, a PV curve is used to assess the
stability at a PQ bus. A PV curve shows the voltage at a given bus, as a function of the
active power demand at that bus. The maximum active power which can be consumed
occurs at the "nose" of the curve where the gradient dV/dP = −∞. After this point, it is
not physically possible to transfer more active power, and an attempt to do so will lead to
voltage instability. For instance, if the load P1 is connected to the PQ bus as shown in
Figure 2.9, this bus is voltage stable, and the bus voltage will be |Vj| = V1. On the other
hand, if the load P2 is connected to the same bus, the TESCA method will detect voltage
instability because there is no bus voltage which corresponds to this load, so V (P2) does
not exist. The shape of the PV curve depends on the system, and thereby the Thévenin
equivalent. A new PV curve is therefore constructed each time the system changes, e.g.
when a line trips.

Zth,j

Figure 2.9: TESCA stability assessment of a PQ bus using a PV curve.
In addition to the aperiodic and voltage instability which the TESCA method can determine,
there can also be cases where the method is simply unable to converge. Such a case could
be where the governors on two generators are "hunting" each other. In this case, there is
no stable operating point, and the TESCA method will therefore be unable to converge.
19
==PAGE:32==
2. Theory
To summarize, the TESCA method can determine four states in which the system can be
in. These are:
• Stable.
• Aperiodically unstable.
• Voltage unstable.
• Unable to converge
When applying the early warning method, one of these four states will be determined for
each investigated scenario.
2.4.6
Complex Power Flow
To analyze the complex power ﬂow in transmission lines in a power system, it is necessary
to know equations which describe this. Such equations will here be given for the two-bus
system shown in Figure 2.10.
X12
I12
|V1|∠δ1
|V2|∠δ2
Figure 2.10: Two-bus system.
Figure 2.10 contains two ideal voltage sources connected through a lossless transmission
line. The line is therefore only represented by a series inductive reactance, X12, which
could be an overhead line. For such a system, the active and reactive power ﬂow in the
line can be described by the following two equations:
P12 = |V1||V2|
X12
sin(δ1 −δ2)
(2.21)
Q12 = |V1|
X12
h
|V1| −|V2| cos(δ1 −δ2)
i
(2.22)
Equation (2.21) shows that the active power ﬂow between two buses is directly proportional
to the sine of the angular diﬀerence between the buses, P12 ∝sin(δ1 −δ2). Therefore, the
real power ﬂow between two buses is mainly determined by the angular diﬀerence in the
terminal voltages of these buses. If V1 leads V2 then sin(δ1 −δ2) is positive, and real power
ﬂows from bus 1 to bus 2. The opposite applies for the case where V1 lags V2.
Equation (2.22) shows how the ﬂow of reactive power is proportional to the diﬀerence
in voltage magnitude between the two buses, Q12 ∝|V1| −|V2|. This equation hereby
shows the close coupling between reactive power and voltage magnitude, which was also
mentioned in Section 2.3.3.
20
==PAGE:33==
2.5. Maximum Line Loading
2.5
Maximum Line Loading
When an electric current ﬂows through a transmission line, heat is dissipated in the line.
This heat increases the conductor temperature which, for an overhead line, causes an
elongation of the conductor. Once this elongation happens, the sag of the conductors
between its support points is increased, and hence the distance from ground to the overhead
line has decreased. Figure 2.11 shows this phenomenon for an overhead line under low and
high line loading conditions.
Figure 2.11: Sag in transmission lines for low and high line loading [8].
As shown in Figure 2.11, the distance from the high loaded overhead line to the tree on
the ground has decreased. For highly loaded lines, in combination with untrimmed trees,
such a scenario may cause line-to-ground faults. Such a fault may cause the tripping of a
line, which could be an initiating disturbance for a cascading outage.
According to [4, p. 226], the general limiting factor in setting the maximum permissible
conductor operating temperature, and thereby also the maximum current for overhead
lines, is the allowable sag of the line. It is therefore necessary to implement overcurrent
protection relays to protect the lines from thermal overload. Such relay models will be
implemented in the PSS/E model.
2.6
Load Modeling
Modeling of power system loads for a time domain simulation are a complex problem.
The loads are usually aggregated and represent multiple devices which all have diﬀerent
characteristics. Such devices may be light bulbs, refrigerators, heaters, compressors, motors,
and furnaces, just to mention some. These devices all behave diﬀerently when exposed to
changes in voltage or frequency. Furthermore, the composition of these devices changes
continuously, depending on the season, time of day, and temperature. Light bulbs will be
more active in the evening than during the day, and heaters will be more active during
winter than summer.
Diﬀerent ways of modeling loads are developed. PSS/E uses the ZIP-model, also called the
polynomial model. By using this load model, the active and reactive power consumption
becomes a function of the bus voltage which the loads are connected to. In the ZIP-model,
the total active and reactive power is divided into three parts. This is seen in the following
21
==PAGE:34==
2. Theory
two equations [9, p. 4]:
P = P0
"
Zp
 V
V0
2
+ Ip
 V
V0

+ Pp
#
(2.23)
Q = Q0
"
Zq
 V
V0
2
+ Iq
 V
V0

+ Qq
#
(2.24)
where:
P, Q = Active/reactive power consumed at operating voltage, V .
P0, Q0 = Active/reactive power consumed at rated voltage, V0.
Zp, Zq = ZIP coeﬃcient representing the constant impedance part.
Ip, Iq = ZIP coeﬃcient representing the constant current part.
Pp, Pq = ZIP coeﬃcient representing the constant power part.
It should be noted that the sum of all ZIP coeﬃcient for P and Q must equal one. This
ensures that P = P0 and Q = Q0 at rated voltage, V = V0. According to [4, p. 273], in
the absence of speciﬁc information it is commonly accepted to represent active power as
constant current loads (Ip = 1) and reactive power as constant impedance loads (Zq = 1).
The power consumption of real loads is also frequency dependent.
For instance the
synchronous speed of an induction motor is directly proportional to the frequency and it is
therefore vulnerable to frequency deviations. The modeling of frequency dependent loads
will, however, not be used in this thesis.

==PAGE:35==
The Early Warning Method 3
The early warning method is a novel method developed by Pauli Fríðheim Petersen for
a PhD project completed at the Technical University of Denmark in December 2016.
The method is able to select the most central branches in a power system, based on a
betweenness centrality approach, and investigate if cascading outages occur following the
disconnection of such branches. Whether cascading outages occur, depends on the power
ﬂow in the system along with the thermal limits of the lines. The early warning method is
able to use either the Newton-Raphson method or the newly developed TESCA method to
calculate this power ﬂow. These methods were described in Section 2.4.
The early warning method is implemented in Matlab where it is designed to analyze a
given power system based on data from the simulation program PSS/E (V.33).
The early warning method takes a set of input as shown in Figure 3.1.
Power system model
PSS/E raw ﬁle
Dynamic models
PSS/E dyr ﬁle
Line lengths
txt ﬁle
User speciﬁed search
depth k, and width x
The Early
Warning
Method
Output scenarios shown
in the Matlab GUI
Figure 3.1: Block diagram showing the input to, and output from, the early warning method. The
dynamic models are only used together with the TESCA method, and hence not necessary when
using the Newton-Raphson method.
The input to the early warning method shown in Figure 3.1 can be described as follows:
• The power system is modeled in a PSS/E raw ﬁle. This ﬁle contains, among others,
the resistances of all lines which are used for calculating the maximum current for
each line.
• As previously mentioned, the early warning method has the possibility of using either
the Newton-Raphson or the newly developed TESCA method for solving the power
ﬂow equations. If the TESCA method is used, it is necessary to specify the dynamic
generator models. These dynamic models are passed onto the early warning method
through a PSS/E dyr ﬁle. This ﬁle is therefore not necessary if the Newton-Raphson
method is used.
23
==PAGE:36==
3. The Early Warning Method
• For the early warning method to be able to calculate the maximum current for each
line, it is necessary to specify the length of each line. This is done by simply passing
a txt ﬁle with the line lengths as input. The line lengths are then used together with
the line resistances given in the raw ﬁle, to calculate the maximum line currents, so
that thermal overload is avoided.
• The early warning method also takes two user-speciﬁed input parameters which
determine the extent of the performed cascading outage assessment. These two
parameters are passed onto the early warning method through a Matlab graphical
user interface (GUI), which will be described in Section 3.2. The two input parameters
are:
– Search depth k: is the parameter used for specifying the number of independent
faults considered. This is expressed as N-k, and this parameter thereby speciﬁes
if e.g. an N-1 or N-2 assessment is to be performed.
– Search width x: speciﬁes the amounts of faults analyzed at each depth.
The input shown in Figure 3.1 is then processed, which results in a set of scenarios, shown in
the Matlab GUI. These scenarios may either be where cascading outages occur following
one or more initial faults or scenarios where the system is determined to be unstable
following the initial fault.
The search depth k and width x can be visualized as shown in Figure 3.2. This ﬁgure shows
that for each added level of search depth, N-1, N-2, N-3 etc., more independent faults are
considered. It is also seen that the search width of the analysis is the number of faults
analyzed at each level of search depth, and x is therefore equal to 3 in this ﬁgure. Following
each line trip, the early warning method calculates a new power ﬂow where the stability of
the system is also assessed. This is shown by the "Static security assessment" boxes. It is
therefore clear how the number of static security assessment calculations increases rapidly
at increased levels of search depth or width. Such increased levels of search depth and
width lead to more detailed cascading outage assessments, but come at the cost of longer
calculation times. The relationship between these will be investigated in Section 5.4.1.
Figure 3.2: Number of fault scenarios investigated at diﬀerent levels of search depth. At N-1 all
possible faults are tested [10, p. 33].
24
==PAGE:37==
3.1. The Early Warning Method Flowchart
3.1
The Early Warning Method Flowchart
The workings of the early warning method algorithm can be described through the ﬂowchart
shown in Figure 3.3, which was presented in [10, p. 32]. This ﬂowchart is divided into
ﬁve stages, which will be described in the following. It should be noted that [10, p. 43]
contains another ﬂowchart where parallelization has been implemented, which increases
the computational speed. It has, however, been decided to show this simpler ﬂowchart, as
it illustrates the principle of the early warning method more clearly.
I Initially, the search depth of the assessment is input to the Matlab GUI through the
kmax variable. This input may e.g. be "2" if an N-2 assessment should be performed.
II Next, the amount of faults considered is input to the Matlab GUI, through the
xmax variable. This stage also performs a check to see if the desired assessment
depth has been reached through the "k ≤kmax?" decision block. If this is the case,
the algorithm ends, and if this is not the case, k is incremented by one. Next, it
is checked if the desired amount of faults has been analyzed at the current level of
search depth through another decision block, "x ≤xmax". If this is the case, the
amount of faults analyzed is reset to zero, and another iteration can be performed
for the next value of k, if k ≤kmax, else the algorithm will end. If on the other hand
this is not the case, the algorithm proceeds to step III to analyze the next fault.
III When the algorithm has entered step III, the most central lines in the system
are computed. This is done using a betweenness centrality approach, which will be
described in Section 3.3. After this computation has been performed, line x is tripped.
Here it should be noted that a new computation of the betweenness centrality of
the lines in the system is performed after each line is tripped. This is necessary
because the topology of the system changes following a line trip, and hence also the
betweenness centrality of the remaining lines in the system.
IV In the previous stage, one of the most central lines in the system was tripped, and
the system is thereafter in a disturbed state. To assess if this state is stable, a "Static
Security Assessment" is performed. This assessment can be performed through either
a Newton-Raphson or TESCA power ﬂow calculation. These methods were described
in Section 2.4.4 and Section 2.4.5, respectively.
If the Newton-Raphson method is used, the "Stability problem" decision box is
answered based on the convergence of the Newton-Raphson method. Using this
method, convergence leads to "N", and no convergence leads to "Y". It should be
noted that the early warning method uses Matpower’s implementation of the
Newton-Raphson method.
If the TESCA method is used, the "Stability problem" will be "Y" if the system is
calculated to be either aperiodically unstable, voltage unstable, or if the calculation
is unable to converge.
If the static security assessment determines that there is a stability problem, the
information regarding this case is saved, and line x + 1 is tripped. If there is not a
stability problem, the algorithm proceeds into stage V.
V The input to this ﬁnal stage of the algorithm is a power system which has been
exposed to at least one line trip, and which has been assessed to be stable through
the "Static Security Assessment". However, the "Static Security Assessment" does not
take maximum line loading limits into consideration, which is accounted for at this

stage. If any line is overloaded, it is tripped, and a new static security assessment is

overloading protection was described in Section 2.5.
3.2
The Graphical User Interface
The early warning method’s Matlab GUI can be seen in Figure 3.4.
Here, an N-2
cascading outage assessment has been executed using the TESCA method with a search
width of 25, as can be seen in the upper left corner of the GUI. In this corner, in addition
to specifying the search depth and width, it is also possible to choose between a weighted
and unweighted betweenness centrality calculation. In [11, p. 1] it has been found that
performing an unweighted betweenness centrality calculation leads to faster computation
times, but it has a negative inﬂuence on the accuracy of the assessment. Therefore, only
the weighted assessment method which will be applied in this thesis.
Figure 3.4: Screenshot of the early warning method’s Matlab GUI using TESCA, performing
an N-2 assessment, with a search width of 25. Case 51 marked by the red box is described in this
section.
27
==PAGE:40==
3. The Early Warning Method
Each row in the table of the GUI represents a case which has started with the initiating
branch disconnection given in the "N-1 Fault" column. After this initial fault, a static
security assessment has been performed, as described in Section 3.1. The outcome of this
static security assessment is given in the "Initial State" column. If the system is assessed to
have a stability problem following the N-1 fault, the type of instability will be given in this
column, denoted by the numbers 2, 3 or 4. These numbers refer to aperiodic instability,
voltage instability, or a case which is unable to converge, respectively.
If the case has been assessed to be stable following the initial N-1 fault, and cascading
outages occur, these are given in the "Cascade" column. After the sequence of cascades
has ended, i.e. there are no more overloaded lines, the new ﬁnal state is written in the
"State" column, which is the outcome of the static security assessment.
If a case has converged successfully after the N-1 fault, and the degree of betweenness
centrality of the N-1 fault branch is suﬃciently high, it is subjected to an N-2 fault. In
Figure 3.4 it is seen that the line 18-19 is subjected to 25 diﬀerent N-2 faults, corresponding
to the selected search width of 25. The 25 lines used for the N-2 faults are those with the
highest degree of betweenness centrality, following the trip of line 18-19.
An example of the information provided by the GUI for case 51 (marked by a red box in
Figure 3.4) is as follows:
1. If line 18-19 is tripped as N-1 fault, the system is stable, marked by "1" in the "Initial
State" column, and no cascading outages occur.
2. If line 17-19 is tripped as an N-2 fault following the N-1 fault trip of line 18-19, the
lines 119-126 and 126-10129 trip in cascade as a result of line overloadings.
3. Following the cascading outages of these two lines, the system is assessed to be
aperiodically unstable, as shown by "2" in the "State" column.
In the following, time domain simulations will be applied to attempt validating this speciﬁc
case and several other cases.
3.3
Betweenness Centrality
The early warning method uses the concept of betweenness centrality to identify branches in
a power system which may cause cascading outages. Betweenness centrality is a part of the
mathematical ﬁeld of graph theory. In graph theory, the layout of a graph/system/network
is subjected to a mathematical analysis. Such a graph may be of various types. These
types range from social, transportation, or communication networks, or power systems, as
used in the early warning method. A network, G, is deﬁned mathematically as G = (V, E),
where V is the set of vertices (hereafter referred to as nodes), and E is the set of edges
(hereafter referred to lines) [12, p. 1]. The concept of weighted and unweighted betweenness
centrality diﬀerentiates between all lines in the network having unique distances, or if
they all have the exact same distance. In power system terms, the term "distance" is the
electrical distance, or impedance, of a given line. As previously mentioned, the unweighted
betweenness centrality has been determined to be faster to calculate, however, it has been
concluded in [11, p. 1] that using the unweighted method is not a good substitute for the
weighted betweenness centrality.
28
==PAGE:41==
3.3. Betweenness Centrality
Betweenness centrality analyzes the importance of the nodes or lines in the system based
on the location of these, which is why the electrical distance is important. Following such
an analysis, a set of numerical values is given for each node or line, which describes the
centrality of these in the system. Transferring the term node and line to power system
terminology, a node could represent a busbar or a substation, and a line could represent a
transmission line.
The betweenness centrality of line e is calculated by Eq. (3.1) [12, p. 2]:
CB(e) =
X
v∈V
X
w∈V \{v}
= σvw(e)
σvw
(3.1)
where σvw(e) is the number of shortest paths between the nodes v and w, which include
the line e, and σvw is the number of shortest paths between v and w. From the numerator
of this equation, it is clear that for a line which is a part of a larger number of shortest
paths between nodes, the line will have a larger betweenness centrality.
The network topology of what could be a power system is shown in Figure 3.5a. In
Figure 3.5b, the level of betweenness centrality has been added for all lines.
(a) Example graph
(b) Edge centrality
Figure 3.5: Example of the betweenness centrality of lines in a system [10, p. 35].
In Figure 3.5b it is seen how the betweenness centrality of the lines in the system varies
from the smallest of 1 (lines EF and FH) to the line with the highest degree of betweenness
centrality of 30 (line IM). The two least central lines in the system could most likely be
disconnected without causing cascading outages in the system. On the other hand, the most
central line, IM, connects all the upper right nodes, with the lower nodes. Disconnecting
line IM would therefore cause all ﬂow from these two areas to go through line BG, which
would then become increasingly loaded. Depending on the capacity of the line and the
change in power ﬂow, this could be the initiating event of a cascading outage. This ﬁgure
hereby illustrates well why the early warning method uses the betweenness centrality
approach to identify lines for the assessment.

==PAGE:42==
The Simulation Model 4
The PSS/E simulation model used in this thesis is an extension of a model developed by
Jia Lou for a master’s thesis completed at the Technical University of Denmark in 2013 [13].
The purpose of that thesis was to construct a model of the Swedish power system, along
with its interconnections to Norway, Denmark, and Finland, to recreate the 2003 Nordic
blackout. This chapter will describe the original model, and the modiﬁcations/extensions
which have been made to make it suitable for this study.
4.1
Model Description
The original PSS/E model contains the transmission level power system of the four countries
Sweden, Norway, Denmark, and Finland. A simpliﬁed view of the way these countries are
interconnected is given in Figure 4.1.
Norway
Sweden
Denmark
Finland
Figure 4.1: Clouds showing how the four countries in the model are interconnected.
The Swedish part of the model is by far the most detailed. The events which lead to
the 2003 Nordic blackout occurred mainly in Sweden, and a detailed model of this country
was therefore necessary for the study performed in [13]. The Swedish part of the model
consists of 182 branches (all overhead lines) 124 buses, 32 generators, 29 transformers,
51 loads and 25 shunt reactors. These components are all at 220 kV or 400 kV level. During
the modeling of the Swedish grid performed in [13], it was not possible to obtain a model
of the system from the Swedish TSO, Svenska Kraftnät. Therefore, for the modeling of the
network topology and determination of transmission line lengths, it was found necessary to
study a map of the Swedish system and construct the system based on measurements on
this map. This approach will inevitably lead to deviations from reality, but it was assessed
30
==PAGE:43==
4.1. Model Description
to be a fair approximation. The map this model has been based on is shown in Figure 4.2,
which also includes the busbar numbering used in the model.
Figure 4.2: Map of the power transmission system in north/western Europe, including the bus
numbers used in the simulation model [13, p. 87].
The main focus for validation of the early warning method is the Swedish part of the model.
This has been chosen since Sweden is the most detailed country, and because it is the only
31
==PAGE:44==
4. The Simulation Model
country where line lengths are speciﬁed in [13]. Therefore, it is only faults, and cascading
outages occurring in Sweden which will be investigated in this thesis.
The Swedish part of the simulation model will be described in the following sections:
• Interconnections to Norway, Denmark and Finland. Section 4.1.1, p. 32.
• Power production. Section 4.1.2, p. 33.
• Power consumption. Section 4.1.3, p. 35.
• Transmission line parameters. Section 4.1.4, p. 35.
4.1.1
Interconnections to Norway, Denmark and Finland
The original simulation model created by Jia Luo contained a detailed model of the
eastern part of Denmark, which was supplied by the Danish TSO, Energinet.dk. For
this thesis, however, the detailed model of Denmark has been removed for conﬁdentiality
reasons. Denmark is therefore represented by a single generator, load and shunt, connected
to the Swedish 400 kV substation Söderåsen through a single line. Denmark exports
approximately 390 MW to Sweden. The aggregated model of Denmark can be seen in
Figure 4.3.
Finland is also modeled in a very simpliﬁed manner. This is performed by two generators,
three loads and two lines connecting to Sweden. Both of these connections are at 400 kV
level and they are in reality connected to the Swedish 400 kV substations Letsi and Svartbyn.
Due to aggregation of substations in the model, these connections do not correspond with
the real substations. Finland exports approximately 609 MW to Sweden. The aggregated
model of Finland can be seen in Figure 4.4.

4.1.2
Power Production
Most of the power produced in Sweden comes from hydro and nuclear power plants. As
of 2017, Sweden has three nuclear power plants in service. These are Ringhals, Oskarshamn
and Forsmark. The rated power from these nuclear power plants is shown in Table 4.1.

In addition to the three nuclear power plants currently in service, the Barsebäck nuclear
power plant has also been in service. However, the two reactors at Barsebäck, each capable
of producing 600 MW were decommissioned in 1999 and 2005 [15]. Even though this thesis
analyses the Swedish grid as it was in the year 2003, the Barsebäck nuclear power plant
will not be included in the model. At the time of the 2003 Nordic blackout, Barsebäck was
out of service due to planned maintenance. Even though a blackout occurred, Svenska
Kraftnät must have believed that the system could remain stable with the Barsebäck
nuclear power plant out of service.
33
==PAGE:46==
4. The Simulation Model
The three nuclear power plants currently in service are all located in the southern parts of
Sweden. A reason for these locations is that most of the power consumption is located in
the southern and central parts of Sweden where the population density is much larger than
in the northern part of the country. In contrast to the nuclear power plants, the hydro
power plants are mainly located in the northern part of Sweden where the large rivers ﬂow.
The location of the three nuclear power plants and the rivers with large amounts of hydro
power plants installed can be seen in Figure 4.6.
Figure 4.6: Location and rated power at the rivers with large hydro power plants installed indicated
by green labels and the four nuclear power plants indicated by yellow labels [16, p. 4].
The Swedish grid is operated in such a way that the nuclear power plants supply a base
production, while the hydro power plants are used for adapting to the variations in demand
which continuously occur. Nuclear power plants have a slow regulating capability and
are therefore not ideal for adapting to these small variations. Consequently they do not
provide frequency support. Due to this grid topology, it is often necessary to transmit
large amounts of power over long distances from the northern hydro power plants to the
many consumers in the south.
There are more than 1000 Swedish hydro power plants installed with ratings ranging from
the kW-area to Sweden’s largest hydro power station, Harsprånget on the Lule River,
34
==PAGE:47==
4.2. Dynamic Model Requirements
which has a power rating of 871 MW [17]. According to Svensk Energi, the total Swedish
production capacity in 2003 was 32,361 MW [Svensk Energi 2004, p.17].
4.1.3
Power Consumption
The total active power consumption in the Swedish part of the PSS/E model is
approximately 16,290 MW. This level of consumption is around half of the maximum
yearly consumption [Svenska Kraftnät 2003a], and the system is therefore considered to be
moderately loaded in the investigated scenario. The largest loads are Stockholm (1742 MW),
Strömma (1515 MW) and Borgvik (1080 MW). These are all located in the southern part
of Sweden. All loads in Sweden have power factors between 0.90-0.95, inductive.
4.1.4
Transmission Line Parameters
When the original model was constructed in [13], it was not possible to obtain any data on
the Swedish transmission lines from the Swedish TSO. Line parameter data was therefore
found elsewhere. Reference [4, p. 209] contains a table with typical overhead transmission
line parameters for diﬀerent voltage levels. The applied transmission line parameters were
determined based on this table and are given in Table 4.2.
Table 4.2: Typical overhead transmission lines parameters used for the transmission lines in the
model, based on [4, p. 209].

The line lengths and electrical properties may be approximated using the map-measurement
