of compensation equipment on the long transmission lines are unknown. Sweden has
transmission lines with lengths of up to 503.5 km [13, p. 84], most likely with some type of
compensation equipment installed. The lack of this compensation equipment in the model
will cause deviations in the results compared to the reality.
4.2
Dynamic Model Requirements
Before the study can be performed, it is necessary to determine which dynamic models
must be included. Firstly, since the study is performed through dynamic time domain
simulations, it is necessary to include dynamic models of the generators as well as their
auxiliary components. These model components are:
• Generator: The dynamic generator model contains, among others, the inertia
constant H, and the transient reactance X′
d. Both were described in Section 2.3.1.2.
This model thereby determines the transient behavior and stability of the generator,
and it is therefore necessary to include such models. The generators in this study
use one of two model types. These are the round rotor model (GENROU), which is
35
==PAGE:48==
4. The Simulation Model
used for the generators at the three nuclear power plants, and the salient pole rotor
model (GENSAL), which is used for the hydro plant generators.
• Exciter: The role of the exciter is to supply and control a direct current to the rotor
windings of the synchronous machine. By controlling this current ﬂow, the terminal
voltage, and reactive power import/export from each generator is controlled. Such a
dynamic model is therefore necessary. Exciters have a large impact on enhancing
the transient stability and must therefore operate very fast in response to system
changes. According to [4, p. 316], modern excitation systems are capable of providing
practically instantaneous response. For this thesis, ﬁve diﬀerent types of exciter
models will be used (IEEET1, IEEET2, EXNEBB, SCRX and SEXS), which were all
chosen in [13], and these models have not been changed for this thesis. These models
in general contain various time constants and gain variables, which determines the
speciﬁc dynamic response of the exciter.
• Turbine governor: As was brieﬂy described in Section 2.3.2, for a generator to
be able to vary its active power output following a disturbance in the system, it
is necessary to have a governor installed. A generator with a governor installed
participates in the primary frequency control of the system by controlling the
mechanical input power to the generator. It is not a requirement that all generators
must have a governor installed to perform a dynamic simulation. The generators
which do not have a governor installed will simply not participate in the primary
frequency control of the system and thereby keep their mechanical input power
constant.
The settings of the governor inﬂuence how fast the generator obtains a new steady
frequency following the disturbance, where a faster settling time most likely would
come at the cost of a larger frequency overshoot. The settings of a governor are
thereby a trade-oﬀbetween these. The governor parameters also include the droop-
characteristic, which determines how much the electric power output changes for a
given change in frequency.
In the original model, most of the hydro plant generators have the hydro-turbine
governor model HYGOV installed, and none of the nuclear power plants has a
governor installed. This has not been changed for this thesis. A reason for not
including governors in the nuclear power plant generators may be that the nuclear
power plants are slow at adjusting their power production compared to the hydro
plants. Another reason may be that the Swedish grid is operated in such a way that
the nuclear power plants supply the base-load in the system while the hydro plants
supply the loads ﬂuctuations.
• Stabilizer: The power system stabilizer (PSS), is used together with the excitation
system to improve the dynamic behavior of a given generator. This is performed,
by adding a damping torque component, which then damps the rotor oscillations
which occur following a disturbance. It is not a requirement for a generator model to
contain a power system stabilizer to perform a dynamic simulation, and not all the
generators in the original model have these installed. This has not been changed for
this thesis.
• Max exciter: The occurrence of cascading outages may very well cause changes in
the system voltages, and hence also the need for reactive power imported/exported
by the generators in the system. If the demand exceeds the generator’s reactive
36
==PAGE:49==
4.3. Model Modiﬁcation and Extension
power capability, it is necessary to have max exciters (OEL) installed to protect the
rotor windings. All generators are equipped with the maximum excitation limiter
model, MAXEX1. This model takes a per-unit maximum ﬁeld voltage as input,
along with a set of ﬁeld voltage/time points. This is used to create a time inverse
operating curve which can disconnect the exciter in the case of overvoltage.
Line overcurrent relay: Since the early warning method calculates if a line is overloaded
and determines if cascading outages occur based on this, it is necessary to include a line
protection model in the dynamic simulation model. This protection model must be able
to trip a line in case of overload. For this purpose, it has been decided to implement
time inverse overcurrent relays. This type of relay trips a given line after the current
ﬂow through it has exceeded a speciﬁed threshold current for a speciﬁed time. The line
overcurrent relays which have been implemented are all of the type TIOCR1.
In addition to the above mentioned models, dynamic models of loads and transformer
tap-changers could also be implemented. However, such models are not necessary for this
study and have therefore been omitted.
From the original model developed by Jia Luo, it was found that all the necessary dynamic
generator models were included.
The settings of these will not be changed for this
study. However, no line protection relays were present in the original model, and these
have therefore been implemented for this study. This implementation is described in
Section 4.3.1.
4.3
Model Modiﬁcation and Extension
It has now been determined that the simulation model contains the necessary dynamic
generator model components, which were deﬁned in Section 4.2. It has also been determined,
that line overcurrent relays must be implemented in the model to be able to simulate
cascading outages. Following this, the model has undergone an extensive investigation to
examine the validity of the model. After having performed this investigation, the following
issues were detected and corrected, so that the model is suﬃciently detailed and suitable
to be used for this study.
• Addition of line overcurrent protection relays, TIOCR1. Section 4.3.1, p. 38.
• Identiﬁcation of substations. Section 4.3.2, p. 45.
• Addition of transmission lines connecting transformers. Section 4.3.3, p. 45.
• Dividing substations into 220 kV and 400 kV. Section 4.3.4, p 48.
• Addition of transformer data. Section 4.3.5, p. 50.
• Addition of missing transmission line. Section 4.3.6, p. 51.
• Addition of new, and increasing existing loads. Section 4.3.7, p. 52.
• Increasing Capacities of Lines Around the Nuclear Power Plants. Section 4.3.8, p. 54.
37
==PAGE:50==
4. The Simulation Model
4.3.1
Addition of Line Overcurrent Protection Relays, TIOCR1
The type of overcurrent relay which has been implemented on the lines in the PSS/E
model is the TIOCR1 model. This model is a Time Inverse OverCurrent Relay which
operates after a user-speciﬁed time inverse operating characteristic. Such a characteristic
is shown in Figure 4.7.
Current [pu]
Time to close relay [sec]
Threshold current
Figure 4.7: Time inverse operating characteristic used in the TIOCR1 models.
Figure 4.7 shows the time it takes for the TIOCR1 model to close its relay as a function
of the current through the line. It is seen in this ﬁgure that the line current must exceed
a speciﬁed threshold current for the line to be able to trip. It is also seen that a larger
violation of the line current will result in a faster relay closing time.
An advantage of using a model with a time inverse operating characteristic is that if the
threshold current has been exceeded, the line will not trip immediately. A speciﬁed time
must pass before the line trips, which is determined by the magnitude of the current
violation. Thereby lines tripping due to high-current transients is avoided. It is not
necessary for the line to disconnect due to such transients, since the conductor temperature
changes very slowly compared to the duration of a current transient. Figure 4.8 shows an
example of how the temperature of a conductor changes over time for a step increase in
conductor current.
38
==PAGE:51==
4.3. Model Modiﬁcation and Extension
Time
Current and temperature
# Step current increase
Conductor current
Conductor temperature
Figure 4.8: Step in conductor current and the corresponding change in the conductor temperature.
Here it is seen how the conductor temperature increases slowly compared to the step in current.
This shows that it is not necessary to trip lines instantly following a current violation to provide
thermal protection.
The TIOCR1 models have been implemented on all lines in Sweden. The lines in Norway,
Denmark, Finland and the interconnections to Sweden are not protected. None of the lines
outside of Sweden is therefore able to disconnect due to overloading. This has been chosen
to limit the study to the Swedish grid.
PSS/E oﬀers the possibility of implementing an overcurrent relay model in both ends of a
transmission line. Due to shunt capacitances along the length of a line, the current entering
and exiting the line will not be the same. The size of this diﬀerence in current increases
as the line length increases. To ensure that the entire length of any line is protected, the
TIOCR1 models have been implemented in both ends of all lines in Sweden.
4.3.1.1
Calculation of Maximum Line Current and Power
To implement the TIOCR1 models, it is necessary to calculate the maximum allowable
line current for each of the protected lines.
As mentioned in Section 2.5, the sag is
the general limiting factor when determining the maximum allowable line current. So
to calculate the maximum line current, it is necessary to choose a maximum allowable
conductor temperature which aﬀects the sag level. The sag is also dependent on the
weather conditions surrounding the conductor, such as the ambient temperature and the
wind speed at the conductor surface. The last parameter considered which aﬀects the
sag is the resistance of the conductor. Heat is dissipated in the resistive part of the line,
and a larger resistance would therefore lead to a higher conductor temperature - for the
same current ﬂow. For two conductors made of the same metal, the conductor with the
largest cross-sectional area has the smallest resistance. However a larger cross-sectional
area is also more expensive, due to the extra metal needed. Diﬀerent metals have diﬀerent
39
==PAGE:52==
4. The Simulation Model
resistivities, where the commonly used aluminum has a higher resistivity than copper, but
is less expensive.
To calculate the maximum allowable conductor current, the "IEEE Standard for Calculating
the Current-Temperature Relationship of Bare Overhead Conductors IEEE Std 738-
2012" [18] has been used. This standard uses the following equation to calculate the
maximum allowable conductor current under steady-state conditions:
Imax =
s
qc + qr −qs
R(Tavg)
(4.1)
where qc is the convection heat loss rate per unit length, qr is the radiated heat loss per
unit length, qs is the heat gain rate from the sun, and R(Tavg) is the DC resistance of the
conductor at temperature Tavg. The calculation of maximum conductor current has been
implemented in a Matlab function, written by Pauli Fríðheim Petersen, which can be
seen in Appendix B.5.
To calculate the maximum allowable conductor temperature, the three weather parameters
mentioned in Table 4.3 must be chosen.
For choosing the maximum allowable conductor temperature and the wind speed at the
conductor, [18, p. 7] recommends 95 ◦C and 0.6 m/s, respectively, as conservative values.
These values are therefore chosen for this thesis. For the ambient temperature, 30 ◦C
has been chosen. Temperatures as high as 38 °C have been measured in Sweden [19],
and it could therefore be argued that the ambient temperature used for the calculations
of maximum line current should be higher. However, such temperatures are extremely
rare. Furthermore, for a country like Sweden which does not have a signiﬁcant amount of
air-conditioning devices installed, it is unlikely that an extremely high temperature would
occur at the same time as a very high power consumption.
The chosen data for calculation of the maximum allowable conductor current has been
collected in Table 4.3.
Table 4.3: Parameters used for the calculation of maximum allowable conductor current.
Description
Value
Based on
Max. allowable conductor temperature
95 ◦C
IEEE Std 738-2012 [18, p. 7]
Wind speed at conductor
0.6 m/s
IEEE Std 738-2012 [18, p. 7]
Ambient air temperature
30 ◦C
Swedish temperature measurements [19]
As explained in Section 4.1, the model of Sweden contains two line types: 220 kV and 400 kV
overhead lines. Since the thermal limit of a conductor depends on the resistance per unit
length and thereby not the total line resistance, the maximum allowable current only needs
to be calculated once for each of the two line types. Using the line resistances given in
Table 4.2 with the Matlab function given in Appendix B.5, the maximum current for the
two line types has been calculated and is shown in Table 4.4. This table also contains the
maximum power transfer for each of these line types.

From the maximum currents in Table 4.4 it is seen that the 400 kV line is capable of
transferring a larger current under the same weather conditions compared with the 220 kV
line. This is due to the smaller line resistance per unit length, which means that the
cross-sectional area of the line is larger and that the Joule heating is smaller, at the same
current ﬂow.
4.3.1.2
Maximum Allowable Line Current Calculation Parameter Sweep
The three weather parameters used for calculating the maximum allowable conductor
current, which were given in Table 4.3, were based on either an IEEE standard or on a
qualiﬁed estimate. It is therefore interesting to investigate how much these parameters
actually inﬂuence the maximum allowable conductor current. By inspecting Eq. (4.1), it is
not straightforward to identify this, and three ﬁgures with a sweep of each parameter have
therefore been constructed. These are shown in Figure 4.9, Figure 4.10 and Figure 4.11.
In all three ﬁgures, it is two of the parameters which ware given in Table 4.3 that have
been used as constants, while the third parameter has been the swept variable in the plot.

In Figure 4.10 it is clearly seen that a relatively small increment in the wind speed leads
to a signiﬁcant increase in the maximum allowable conductor current. Since the diﬀerence
between using e.g. 1 m/s or 2 m/s is approximately 300 A for a 400 kV line, using a diﬀerent
wind speed in the model, compared with what was used by the Swedish TSO when
designing the relays, will most likely lead to signiﬁcant deviations from reality. However,
in this context, it should be mentioned that distance/impedance relays are most likely
implemented in the Swedish system. A distance relay has the advantage over a simple
overcurrent relay that it can calculate the location of a line fault based on a voltage/current
measurement. Consequently, this type of relay also oﬀers a better way of coordinating
between multiple relays. It should be noted that the sudden increase in current which occurs
at approximately 0.19 m/s is caused by the way IEEE Std 738-2012 [18] calculates the
maximum conductor current. The standard provides equations which diﬀerentiate between
high/low wind speed and forced/natural convection. This diﬀerentiation is implemented as
if-statements in the Matlab function, Appendix B.5, which causes the sudden increase
at approximately 0.18 m/s.
Figure 4.11 shows that an increase in maximum conductor temperature of 10 °C leads
to an increase in maximum allowable conductor current of 172 A, for a 400 kV line. As
a reference, aluminum has a melting temperature of 660 °C [20]. However, as previously
mentioned it is the sag which is the limiting factor and not the risk of a line melting.

4.3.1.3
PSS/E Implementation of the TIOCR1 Overcurrent Relays
To implement a TIOCR1 model in PSS/E, the following data must be speciﬁed [21, p. 11-26]:
• A threshold current in per-unit on system base,
which for this study is
Sbase = 1000 MVA. The threshold currents have been calculated using the Matlab
function shown in Appendix B.5, resulting in the currents given in Table 4.4.
• Five current/time points, which creates a time inverse operating characteristic as
shown in Figure 4.7. The currents speciﬁed in these points are multiples of the
threshold current.
• A breaker time, which is the time it takes for the line to trip after the relay has
closed. This breaker time is thereby independent of the magnitude of the current
ﬂow in the line.
Since the time inverse characteristic is constructed based on a set of ﬁve discrete
current/time points, it is possible to specify this characteristic as desired, and many
diﬀerent standards exist for doing so. In general, these all follow the same principle, the
diﬀerence being the slope of the curve. An example of three of such curves is shown in
Figure 4.12.
43
==PAGE:56==
4. The Simulation Model
Current [pu]
Time to close relay [sec]
Threshold current
Moderately inverse
Very inverse
Extremely inverse
Figure 4.12: Three types of time inverse operating characteristics.
As Figure 4.12 shows, all three characteristics cause the relay to close for a given current
above the threshold current. The main diﬀerence between the curves is the time it takes
for this to happen. Choosing which curve to use in a given situation may for instance be
done based on system protection coordination considerations. For this study, however, all
the TIOCR1 models will be of the same type, and no coordination is taken into account.
The curve is chosen to be rather steep, since this reduces the simulation time. The chosen
curve is the "US CO2 Short Time Inverse" [22, p. 9-4]. This curve can mathematically be
described by the following function:
t(Ir) = 0.02394
I0.02
r
−1 + 0.01694
where
Ir = Imeasured/Ithreshold
(4.2)
Since the PSS/E TIOCR1 model takes a set of discrete points on the time inverse
characteristic curve as input, it is necessary to deﬁne such points from the function
given in Eq. (4.2). A set of appropriate points has been calculated in Matlab, which
results in the following characteristic, shown in Figure 4.13.
Figure 4.13 shows that even though the TIOCR1 model takes a set of discrete points as
input rather than the actual mathematical function deﬁned in Eq. (4.2), there is only a
slight mismatch between the two.
To implement the TIOCR1 models in PSS/E, a Matlab function shown in Appendix B.18,
has been constructed. This function uses the from-bus, to-bus and maximum current for
each line to create a txt ﬁle with the TIOCR1 relay settings, which can be added to the
dyr ﬁle used in the model.

4.3.2
Identiﬁcation of Substations
The majority of the busbars in the original model were not labeled by the real name of the
substation which they represent. Instead, the busbars were labeled after their geographical
location, e.g. "NORTH_X" and "CENTRAL_X". A reason for this way of labeling may be
that many of the minor substations and their connecting lines had been aggregated, and it
was therefore not possible to name them according to reality. It has been deemed necessary
to identify as many substations as possible to obtain an overview of the model compared
with reality. The identiﬁcation of substations in the model has been performed based on a
map of the European transmission network, created by ENTSO-E [23]. However, due to
the substation aggregation, it has not been possible to identify all substations, and some
busbars are therefore stilled named "NORTH_X" or "CENTRAL_X".
4.3.3
Addition of Transmission Lines Connecting Transformers
By inspecting the single line diagram of Sweden, it was found that all connections between
buses of diﬀerent voltage levels were performed by a transformer without a line model. Three
examples of such connections between 220 kV and 400 kV buses are shown in Figure 4.14.
These buses are geographically apart from each other. For instance, the physical line
connecting bus 28 to bus 32 is 28.5 km [13, p. 84]. There is, however, no line connecting
these buses, as shown in Figure 4.14 - there is only a transformer.

==PAGE:59==
4.3. Model Modiﬁcation and Extension
does in fact contain the line series impedance. According to the standard PSS/E model for
a two-winding transformer shown in Figure 4.15, the transformers are thereby modeled as
ideal transformers with the equivalent transformer impedance, ¯Zeq = ¯req + j¯xeq, actually
being the line impedance [24, p. 4-9]. The eﬀect each transformer has on the system is
thereby only the voltage transformation and the tap-changing capability. No transformer
losses are included, since the transformer impedance has been neglected in favour of the
line impedance.
↑Line impedance
Figure 4.15: Standard PSS/E two-winding transformer circuit [24, p. 4-9] where the equivalent
transformer impedance has been used to model the line impedance which connects the transformer.
This way of modeling transformers and lines in a single PSS/E transformer model may
have been suﬃcient for the type of study which the model was originally developed for.
However, for this study it is necessary to include individual models of all the lines. This is
due to the fact that tripping of lines due to overloading is of main concern, and it must
therefore be possible to implement line protection relays on all lines in the system.
To implement the missing lines, a new busbar along with a new line has been added at all
transformers in Sweden. This is done by the modeling approach shown in Figure 4.16b,
which is shown next to the original modeling approach in Figure 4.16a.
(a) Original transformer mod-

(b) New transformer modeling

has been added.

New lines and busbars have been added throughout the Swedish part of the model, using
the modeling approach shown in Figure 4.16b. For comparison, the part of the model
which was shown in Figure 4.14 is now modeled as shown in Figure 4.17. The voltage level
of the added lines were found in [13, p. 84-86].

4.3.4
Dividing Substations into 220 kV and 400 kV Busbars
In the original model, all the Swedish substations were represented by a single busbar at
either 220 kV or 400 kV level. However, from the map in Figure 4.2, it is clear that while
some substations only have connections at one voltage level, other substations contain
connections with transmission lines of both voltage levels. An example of this is shown in
Figure 4.18 where e.g. Odensala is a 400 kV substation, while Stockholm, Kolbotten and
Ekudden contains both 220 kV and 400 kV connections.

In Figure 4.19 it is shown how the substations Kolbotten, Stockholm and Ekudden are all
modeled as a single 400 kV busbars in the original model. The transformers connecting
Kolbotten-Stockholm and Ekudden-Stockholm both have a ratio of 1:1, connected to 400 kV
busbars at both terminals. The transformers thereby only represent the transmission line
series impedance, as described in Section 4.3.3, along with the tap-changing capability of
the transformer. This way of modeling the transformers and lines together leads to the
problem that e.g. the 220 kV lines connecting Kolbotten to Stockholm were modeled as
400 kV lines. According to Table 4.4, such a line has a higher maximum current transfer
capability, and not modeling it as a 220 kV line would lead to incorrect settings of the
TIOCR1 overcurrent protection relays, and hence unrealistic simulation results.
The substations which contained connections of both 220 kV and 400 kV levels have been
modiﬁed, so that each of these substations now consists of two busbars, connected by
a single transformer. In reality, a number of parallel transformers would most likely be
implemented to add redundancy in the system, and/or due to ﬁnancial considerations.
Such considerations has however not been made in this study, and no parallel transformers
are implemented. The updated version of the PSS/E model shown in Figure 4.19 is shown
in Figure 4.20.
This way of modifying the busbars in the substations has been performed on a number of
substations throughout the model.

4.3.5
Addition of Transformer Data
Due to the previously described modeling approach of the transformers, no information
regarding the impedances of these is known. It has therefore been decided to model
the transformers based on standard values. The 2-winding PSS/E transformer model
takes both a series impedance consisting of a resistance and reactance part as well as a
magnetizing admittance as input. This was shown in Figure 4.15 where ¯Zeq = ¯req + j¯xeq.
The choice of these parameters will now be described.
Magnetizing admittance: According to [25, p. 15] this admittance can be neglected in
most cases, unless the core is saturated. Based on this, the magnetizing admittance will
not be included in the transformer models in this study.
Series reactance: According to a Cigré report, a realistic rating for a transformer short
circuit reactance is 10 % [26, p. 295]. This value will therefore be used on the transformers
in the model. Before this value can be entered in the PSS/E transformer models, it needs
to be calculated to a per-unit value, which is based on the transformer and system base
power. This is performed using the following equation:
Xpu = ex
100 · Ssyst.base
Strf
·
U2
trf
U2
syst.base
(4.5)
Before Eq. (4.5) can be applied, it is necessary to determine the rated power of the
transformers. After having investigated the system, it has been determined that all the
transformers should have a rated power of 1000 MVA, with the previously determined short
circuit reactance of 10 %. If bottlenecks in the system are to be completely avoided, some
of the transformer ratings must be larger than this. However, this rating has been deemed
to be suﬃcient. It should be noted that transformers of this size may not be realistic, but
due to lack of information it has been found suﬃcient for this study.
50
==PAGE:63==
4.3. Model Modiﬁcation and Extension
The per-unit series reactance to be input in the PSS/E transformer model is then calculated
using Eq. (4.5) as follows:
Xpu,1000MVA = 10% · 1000 MVA
1000 MVA · (220 kV)2
(220 kV)2 = 0.1 pu
(4.6)
Equation (4.6) shows that the transformer reactance does not change when converted to
the system power base, which is of course the case as the base powers are equal.
Series resistance: For high power transformers the series resistance is often neglected.
As the transformers become larger, the inductance increases at a faster rate than the
resistance. One reason for this is that the windings are made of thicker wire, which reduces
the resistance. According to [25, p. 63], the X/R ratio for power transformers can be
between 15 and 125, with the highest ratios for the larger transformers. However, for this
study it has been decided to implement the transformer resistance to make the model
more detailed. Based on the information given in [25, p. 63] regarding transformer X/R
ratios, a ratio of 50 has been chosen. This is a rough estimate, however, due to lack of
information, it has been deemed to be suﬃcient.
X/R ratio = Xpu,1000MVA
Rpu,1000MVA
= 50
(4.7)
The per-unit series resistance to be input in the PSS/E transformer model is calculated by:
Rpu,1000MVA = Xpu,1000MVA
X/R ratio
= 0.1 pu
50
= 0.002 pu
(4.8)
This concludes the collection of data used for the Swedish transformer models.
4.3.6
Addition of Missing Transmission Line
The next part of the model which was investigated were the transmission lines in Sweden.
To perform an analysis which is as realistic as possible, it is important to have all
transmission lines included in the PSS/E model. Depending on the geographical location
of the transmission lines, the system would most likely become more robust with additional
lines, since loading is distributed among more lines. Identifying if any lines are missing is
done by comparing the single line diagram of the original PSS/E model with a map of the
Swedish power system as it was in 2003. The most ideal set-up would be to use the map in
Figure 4.2, as this is the map the original PSS/E model was based on. This has, however,
not been possible in the crowded area around Stockholm, due to the low resolution of this
map. Another map has therefore also been used, which shows the southern part of Sweden
at a slightly better resolution. This map is shown in Figure 4.21.
51
==PAGE:64==
4. The Simulation Model
Figure 4.21: The Southern part of the Swedish power system. The red box shows the double line
system from Forsmark to Odensala where a new line has been added to the original model. Map
from Nordel.
From Figure 4.21 it can be seen that the connection between the Forsmark nuclear power
plant south of the Odensala substation is a double line system. This double line system is
marked by the red box in the ﬁgure. The connection between these two substations in
the original model is only a single line, and it is therefore concluded that an additional
line must be added in parallel. The lack of such a line may be critical due to the large
power production capability from the Forsmark nuclear power plant of 3291 MW [14]. The
load which represents Stockholm has a power consumption of 1742 MW, power factor 0.95
lagging. Due to the size of this load, any additional power transfer capability from Forsmark
towards Stockholm,is much desirable.
Except for the missing Forsmark-Odensala line, no other missing lines have been identiﬁed.
4.3.7
Addition of New and Increasing Existing Loads
As shown in Section 4.1.3, the total active power consumption in Sweden in the original
model is approximately 16,290 MW. By performing simulations, it was found that many of
the lines in the system were only lightly loaded, with several loadings in the range of 5 %-
15 %. The system is hence very robust towards line overcurrents in such a moderately
loaded system, and there were few line outage scenarios which could cause cascading
outages. Since the purpose of the thesis is to validate the scenarios which the early warning
52
==PAGE:65==
4.3. Model Modiﬁcation and Extension
method identiﬁes to cause cascading outages, it was found necessary to increase the power
ﬂow in the system, and hence the number of cascading outage scenarios. New loads
have therefore been added, and the power consumption of some existing loads has been
increased.
Addition of new loads: The original model contained multiple buses which were only
connected to a single line, making the eﬀect of such a line/bus negligible. Examples of such
three buses (Juktan, Stalon and Bagede) are shown in Figure 4.22a. A load is most likely
present at each substation in the real system, and it is therefore believed that these loads
have been neglected in the original model due to their small size. Since no information
is given in [13] regarding the missing loads, it has been decided to add a load of 50 MW,
power factor 0.95 lagging, on all buses which only have a single line connection. Such a
load has been added to 13 buses throughout Sweden, leading to a total additional active
power consumption of 650 MW, and reactive power consumption of 312 Mvar. Figure 4.23
shows how three new loads have been added to the Juktan, Stalon and Bagede stations.

Increasing existing loads: In addition to the 13 new loads which have been added to
the model, some of the existing loads have also been increased. This load increase has
been implemented on loads located in the southern part of Sweden. This has been chosen
since it will increase the loading on the long lines transporting power from the hydro
plants in the north. The hydro generators in the north have therefore been set to produce
the additional power. None of the generators in the southern part of Sweden has been
changed. The following loads have been increased by 25 % on both active and reactive
power consumption: Valbo (43), Lindbacka (71), Borgvik (89), Strömna (93), Breared
(96), Alvesta (100), Nybro (102), Karlshamm (103), Sege (106) and Stockholm (110). This
has lead to a total increase in active power of 708 MW.
After these load changes have been implemented, the total active power consumption in
Sweden is 17,648 MW, which is an increase of approximately 8 % compared to the original
level of consumption. Despite the relatively small size of this load increase, the location of
53
==PAGE:66==
4. The Simulation Model
the increased loads in the south and the increased power production in the north have
signiﬁcantly increased the loading on many of the northern lines, making several cascading
outages scenarios possible.

The ﬁnal investigation which has been performed is of the line loadings in Sweden. To do
this, the contour plot function in PSS/E was applied. A contour plot has been made for
the initial line loadings, which is shown in Figure 4.23a. The contour settings for this plot
are 0 % and 100 % for minimum and maximum line loading, respectively. The maximum
line loading is based on the calculated maximum power transfer, as shown in Table 4.4.

With line loadings as high as those shown in Table 4.5, a single line trip of either the
Forsmark-Tuna, Ringhals-Strömma or Oskarshamn-Nybro line would cause a cascading
outage of the remaining lines connected to that power plant. This would eventually lead to
a system blackout. It is not realistic that the Swedish system is constructed/operated in
such a way that a blackout could occur following a single loss of one of these transmission
lines. It can therefore be concluded that the rated current of these lines must be increased,
as this is most likely the case in the real Swedish system. An argument for safely increasing
the maximum line current is that all three nuclear power plants are located at the seashore,
and the wind speed at such a location will most likely be higher than the wind speed
farther in the country. When calculating the maximum allowable conductor current, it
was found in Figure 4.10 that a relatively small increase in the wind speed surrounding
the lines causes a relatively large increase in maximum allowable current. Therefore, the
maximum allowable current, and hence the also the maximum power transfer capability
for the lines given in Table 4.5, has been increased by 50 % compared with the values
which were given in Table 4.4. This is equivalent to calculating the maximum allowable
conductor current with a wind speed of approximately 2.8 m/s, rather than the 0.6 m/s
which is used for the other lines. A new contour plot of the line loadings with the increased
line capacity can be seen in Figure 4.23b.

4.5
Initial Load Flow Analysis
After the described modiﬁcations and extensions have been implemented in the model, an
initial load ﬂow calculation has been executed. This load ﬂow calculation is executed with
Ligga (bus 9) being the slack bus. This bus is located in the northern part of Sweden.
Ligga was chosen as slack bus in [13, p. 41] and this has not been changed for this study.
The initial load ﬂow calculation determines that the slack bus generator produces:
Pslack = 246.3 MW
Qslack = −104.2 Mvar
The slack bus generator has a maximum active power production of 526 MW, and a
maximum reactive power import/export of ±263 Mvar.
Consequently, the slack bus
generator is thereby well within operating limits.
4.5.1
Initial Line Loadings
The initial line loadings are shown in Figure 4.26.
This ﬁgure shows that there are
no heavily loaded lines, which seems realistic since the system is moderately loaded, as
mentioned in Section 4.1.3. It is also seen how the lines in the southern part of Sweden are
generally only mildly loaded, while the lines in the northern part are more loaded. This
line loading load distribution is as it was intended when existing loads were increased, as
described in Section 4.3.7.
Figure 4.26: Contour plot of the initial line loadings in Sweden. Here it is seen that no lines are
heavily loaded, and that the lines in the northern part of Sweden generally are loaded more than in
the southern part. This load distribution allows for more cascading outage scenarios.

4.5. Initial Load Flow Analysis
4.5.2
Initial Bus Voltages
A contour plot of the per-unit bus voltage magnitudes in Sweden has also been constructed.
This is shown in Figure 4.27a where the lower and upper limits are 0.9 pu and 1.1 pu,
respectively. This ﬁgure shows how the voltage at the three nuclear power plants is slightly
orange and thereby above 1 pu. The reason for this is that the scheduled voltage at these
three buses is set to 1.03 pu. The minimum voltage in Sweden is at CENTRAL_80, located
in the southern part of Sweden. This bus has a voltage magnitude of 0.935 pu.
The voltage angles of the buses in the system have also been plotted in a contour plot,
which can be seen in Figure 4.27b. This contour plot has been made with a lower and
upper angle limit of −67° and 8°, respectively. These angles have been chosen as they
represent the minimum and maximum angles in Sweden, following the initial load ﬂow
calculation. By choosing these angles, the contour plot will be as detailed as possible, in
comparison to having chosen e.g. ±90°.
(a) Bus voltage magnitudes.
(b) Bus voltage angles.
Figure 4.27: Contour plots of the complex bus voltages in Sweden after the initial load ﬂow
calculation.
Figure 4.27b clearly shows how the buses in the northern part of Sweden have larger angles
than in the southern part, with Ritsem (bus 1) being the largest at an angle of 7.71°.
The voltage angles decrease from this point towards south, with the smallest angle at
CENTRAL_80, which has an angle of −66.24°. According to Eq. (2.21) it can be concluded
from these angles that the active power transfer generally goes from north to south. This
power ﬂow direction is as desired, since it will add loading on the long lines connecting
these parts of the country, thus allowing for more cascading outage scenarios.

==PAGE:72==
Test and Validation of the
Early Warning Method 5
The early warning method has been applied on the modiﬁed and extended PSS/E model
to perform an N-1 and N-2 assessment, using both the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA
method for static security assessment. These assessments resulted in a set of scenarios for
each of the two methods, where cascading outages and/or system instability is predicted.
The identiﬁed scenarios will be validated in this chapter through time domain simulations
performed in PSS/E.
It will also be investigated if the early warning method identiﬁes an N-1 and N-2 cascading
outage scenario, which is known in advance. This is another way of validating the early
warning method.
5.1
Simulation Initialization and Procedure
All simulations have been executed in PSS/E using the following initialization and
simulation procedure:
• All simulations are performed with the dynamic simulation option "Network frequency
dependence" activated. This makes the following components frequency dependent:
line reactances and capacitances, generator source reactances, shunts and reactive
loads modeled as constant impedance [27, p. 24-4].
According to [27, p. 24-4],
this option should be activated when frequency
"substantially deviates from nominal". The drawback of using this feature is that it
may add 10 % to 20 % to the simulation time. Even though the frequency deviations
are modest in this project, it has been decided to activate this feature since the
simulation time is not an issue.
• All loads are modeled with ZIP coeﬃcients (described in Section 2.6) of Pz = 0.35,
Pi = 0.4 , Pp = 0.25, Qz = 1, Qi = 0, and Qp = 0. These values were determined
in [13, p. 61] to be a reasonable approximation of the loads in the real Swedish
system.
• N-1 line trips are applied at t = 1 s.
• N-2 line trips are applied at t = 30 s.
• In all stable scenarios, the simulation has been executed until all TIOCR1 pickup
timers have been reset. This ensures that a new steady-state has been reached, where
no additional line trips can occur.
• In all unstable scenarios, the simulation has been executed until there is a clear
indication of instability. The process of determining this is described in Section 5.2.
60
==PAGE:73==
5.2. Stability Assessment
• Generator 7000 has been used as reference machine for all rotor angles in the system.
This machine has been chosen because it is the machine with the largest inertia in
the system.
The level of overloading on the transmission lines in the system varies signiﬁcantly in the
diﬀerent scenarios, and simulation times between 20 - 3800 seconds have been used.
The output window of PSS/E following the initialization of the dynamic simulation can
be seen in Appendix C. Here it is seen that the model initializes without any warnings or
errors and that the slack bus produces the active and reactive power which was stated in
Section 4.5.
5.2
Stability Assessment
For each of the simulations executed in PSS/E, the same variables have been extracted
and imported into Matlab. The import of this data is performed through the developed
Matlab function loadScenario, which is shown in Appendix B.6.
The following is a brief description of these variables and how the information they provide
has been used.
• Generator rotor angles (with generator at bus 7000 as reference). As it will be
shown in the upcoming analysis, a general mechanism causing instability following
line trips is the loss of synchronism of generators in the system. Once a generator
loses synchronism, it is seen in the rotor angle, which begins a continuous excursion,
either positive or negative, away from the remaining generator rotor angles. In the
PSS/E simulations, this angle increases for the entire duration of the simulation and
can reach angles of several thousand degrees, depending on the simulation time. Such
angles are obviously not physically possible, and furthermore, protection devices
would most likely disconnect a generator which loses synchronism. However, such
protection is not implemented in this model. The rotor angle variables are therefore
only used to indicate if loss of synchronism occurs for a given generator, and if the
rotor angle increases positively or negatively. The magnitude of the angle is not used
in the analysis.
The rotor angles can also be used to identify if a system split has occurred. In such
a case, the rotor angles of the split groups will continuously increase their angle from
each other. An example of this is given in Section 5.3.2 on Transient Stability.
The reason for the loss of synchronism following a line trip was described in
Section 2.3.1.2, on page 7.
• Generator speed deviation. In steady-state all the generators are operating at
nominal speed, and the speed deviation is therefore zero. When a disturbance occurs
in the system, the impedance seen from each generator changes, and so do the
rotor angles. The speed deviation of the rotor is the time derivative of the rotor
angle, and a speed deviation therefore occurs. This was explained in more detail
in Section 2.3.1.2, on Transient Stability. Should a system split occur, this can be
seen as two groups of generators which operate at diﬀerent speed deviations. This
variable can then be used to identify if such a split has occurred.
The generator speed deviation in collaboration with the generator rotor angle for a
61
==PAGE:74==
5. Test and Validation of the Early Warning Method
given generator can create a state-space plot, which provides good insight into the
behavior of a given generator, following a disturbance. Such a state-space plot is
shown in Figure 5.6.
• Generator ﬁeld voltage. The generator’s ﬁeld voltage controls the reactive power
import/export from a given generator. This reactive power plays a signiﬁcant role
in controlling the terminal voltage of a generator. A change of reactive power ﬂow
from a given generator following a disturbance, thereby indicates that the voltage at
that bus would have deviated from nominal if the reactive power capability of the
generator was not available. This variable can also be used to see if an overexcitation
limiter (OEL) has activated.
• Generator electrical power. This variable can be used together with the generator
mechanical input power to investigate if there is a mismatch between the two. This
would indicate that an acceleration in rotor angle has occurred.
• Generator mechanical power.
In addition to what was mentioned for the
generator electrical power, this variable can be used to see the eﬀect of the governors,
where the mechanical power changes following a disturbance if a governor is activated.
• Bus frequency. PSS/E oﬀers the possibility of extracting the frequency at all buses
in the system. In steady-state, this frequency will be the same at all buses, and these
variables can therefore be used to validate the stability of a system.
It is possible that the steady-state frequency deviates from nominal following a
disturbance. In the case of over frequency, load has been disconnected, causing
a reduction in the demand for electrical power. In the case of under frequency,
additional load has been added, which could be the increasing loss on transmission
lines following line trips. If a system split has occurred, this can be shown by the
frequency at two groups of buses being the same and diﬀerent from each other. An
example of this is shown in Figure 5.11.
• Bus complex voltages. The bus voltage magnitudes can be used to identify if
voltage instability has occurred. The diﬀerence in voltage magnitude between two
buses can also be used to show the direction of the reactive power ﬂow.
The bus voltage angles can be used to see if loss of synchronism has occurred. It can
also be used to determine the direction of active power ﬂow between two buses.
Equations for this were given in Section 2.4.6.
• Line complex power ﬂow (P and Q). The magnitude of the complex power ﬂow
in a line is proportional with the magnitude of the current ﬂowing in that line. The
active and reactive power ﬂow variables are therefore used to determine the loading
on the lines in the system. PSS/E calculates the active and reactive power ﬂow
individually, and the magnitude of the complex power ﬂow has then been calculated
in Matlab, for this purpose. The maximum line loading for the 220 kV and 400 kV
lines were given in Table 4.4.
To assess the stability of such a large system with thousands of variables, it has been found
expedient to construct a Matlab function which provides an overview of the system’s state
during the course of a simulation. All the previously mentioned variables have therefore
been plotted into a single ﬁgure, using the developed Matlab function, plotScenario
given in Appendix B.14. Figure 5.1 shows an example of an N-1 scenario where line 131-132
trips.
62
==PAGE:75==
5.2. Stability Assessment
Figure 5.1: Screenshot of the plotScenario Matlab function, which is used to assess the
stability of the system following a line trip. The line trip applied in this simulation is the N-1 trip
of line 131-132.
Another Matlab function which plots line loadings has also been constructed. This
function can be found in Appendix B.13. The function is used to show the loading of the
lines which are tripped manually in PSS/E and the lines predicted by the early warning
method to trip. An example of the use of this function is shown in Figure 5.2 for the N-2
scenario where the lines 89-91 and 18-131 trip at t = 1 s and t = 30 s, respectively. The
lines 119-126, 126-10129, 73-130 and 36-37 are predicted by the early warning method to
trip. However, this does not occur.
By creating a plot as shown in Figure 5.2 for all simulation scenarios it is conﬁrmed that the
desired N-k lines actually trip, and they do so at the speciﬁed times deﬁned in Section 5.1.
Furthermore, it is ensured that all the lines predicted to trip by the early warning method
have either tripped or reached a new steady-state loading level.

5.3
Known Cascading Outage Scenarios
A way of validating the early warning method is by investigating if the method detects a
scenario which is known in advance to cause cascading outages. Such two scenarios for an
N-1 and N-2 case have been manually identiﬁed in the PSS/E model. These scenarios have
been identiﬁed by investigating the network topology, the direction of power ﬂow and level
of line loadings. Hereafter, time domain simulations with line trip events were executed
based on qualiﬁed assumptions until two cascading outage scenarios were identiﬁed.
The two known cascading outage scenarios will be analyzed in detail in this section to
illustrate how the system behaves and which stability mechanisms occur in such scenarios.
5.3.1
Known N-1 Scenario (Line 51-75)
To identify an N-1 cascading outage scenario, the line loading contour plot shown in
Figure 4.26 was utilized. This ﬁgure was searched for two lines which were connected
to the same busbar in one end, was loaded above 50 %, and was transferring power in
the same direction. When tripping one of such two lines, there is a high chance of the
other line tripping as a consequence of this. Using this approach, the two 220 kV lines
Finslatten(51)-CENTRAL_75 and Finslatten(51)-CENTRAL_76 were found. These lines
have initial line loadings of 58 % and 69 %, respectively. Since both lines have the same
rated power (Table 4.4) and are loaded above 50 %, it is likely that tripping one of the lines
will cause the other one to become overloaded and eventually trip following this initial
disturbance.
64
==PAGE:77==
5.3. Known Cascading Outage Scenarios
It has been decided that the initial N-1 trip shall be of the Finslatten(51)-CENTRAL_75
line. This has been simulated, and a timeline of selected events which occur is shown in
Table 5.1.

buses shown in Table 5.1, and reaches a peak value of 50.42 Hz. The loads connected to
the isolated buses have a total active power consumption of 824 MW, and disconnecting
these buses thereby creates an immediate excess of active power in the system. This is
what causes the increase in frequency. Once the frequency deviates from nominal, it is
detected by the governors in the system, which begin to decrease the mechanical input
power to the generators, participating in the primary frequency control. This causes the
frequency to reach a new steady-state level.

have a governor installed and participates in the primary frequency control. Figure 5.5
clearly shows how the governor at bus 7100 decreases the mechanical input power to the
generator, following the disturbance which caused an excess of active power in the system.

Figure 5.5: Mechanical input power to the generators connected to bus 7000 and bus 7100. Here it

It takes approximately 90 seconds from the frequency increase initiates before the governors
on the generators decrease the mechanical power input and reach a new steady-state
frequency. At 50.09 Hz, this frequency is slightly higher than nominal. For the frequency
to return to nominal it would be necessary to have generators with secondary control
implemented in the model. These generators would decrease their mechanical input power
in an over-frequency situation. This would cause the generators only participating in
primary frequency control to follow along, which would restore nominal frequency.

==PAGE:79==
5.3. Known Cascading Outage Scenarios
Following the line trips, the reactances seen from each generator have changed. So has the
mechanical input power to the generators which has governors installed. It was also shown
in Figure 5.4 that the frequency has changed and now deviates from the nominal 50 Hz.
It is therefore likely that the generator rotor angles in the system has also changed.
To investigate the way this change happens, a state-space representation of the rotor
angle and speed deviation for the slack bus generator has been created, which can be
seen in Figure 5.6. This ﬁgure shows how the generator initially operates at zero speed
deviation due to the nominal frequency. Once the ﬁrst line trip disturbance occurs, the
generator speed increases, and the rotor angle increases. The rotor angle increases to
approximately −15.45° before it begins to oscillate in the other direction. The theory
behind this was described in Section 2.3.1.2. Following the transient course, the generator
reaches a new steady-state with a positive speed deviation. This means that the generator
runs faster than nominal speed, which creates a higher frequency, just as expected based
on Figure 5.4.

5.3.2
Known N-2 Scenario (Line 36-39 and 34-36)
Using the line loading contour plot in Figure 4.26, an N-2 cascading outage scenario
has been identiﬁed. When identifying this scenario, it was interesting to see if it was
possible to identify a scenario which the early warning method did not detect. Therefore,
Figure 4.26 was used together with the identiﬁed N-2 cascading outage scenarios, which
will be presented in Section 5.4.3. It was found rather diﬃcult to identify such a scenario.
All scenarios which seemed likely to cause cascading outages were identiﬁed by the early
warning method. Identifying a scenario which was unidentiﬁed by the early warning method
therefore required several simulation attempts. Because of the diﬃculty of identifying
such a scenario, it was not possible to identify one where more than one cascading outage
occurs. Such a scenario is, however, still detectable by the early warning method, and the
scenario is therefore applicable for this validation.
The known N-2 scenario consists of the N-1 trip of line Hjalta(36)-Tuggen(39), and the
N-2 trip of line Stornorrfors(34)-Hjalta(36). The location of these lines can be seen in
Figure 5.8.

To further investigate the system split, it is interesting to see where the split occur, on
a map. Such a map is shown in Figure 5.12, where a black line divides Sweden into two
areas. Before any disturbances are applied on the system, there are ﬁve lines connecting
these two areas (3-12, 34-36, 35-44, 36-39 and 5-130). However, three of these lines are

lines are very long and consequently they have high impedances. This reduces the transfer
capability of the generators from north to south, which causes the split. The generators
closest to the area-connecting line for both areas are marked on the map by means of a
violet and blue box. These boxes show that the generators at Ligga and Stornorrfors are
in synchronism and out of synchronism with the generators at Grundfors and Valbo, which
are in synchronism. This corresponds with Figure 5.10 and Figure 5.11. Figure 5.12 also
shows the distance between the generators in the split areas, which is quite long.

it has already been revealed, the early warning method does not identify the known N-2
scenario. The reason for this will be investigated in Section 5.5.2.

5.4. Early Warning Method Identiﬁed Scenarios
5.4
Early Warning Method Identiﬁed Scenarios
This section will describe the application, and show the results from the early warning
method. The method has been used for an N-1 and N-2 assessment, using both the
Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method. The identiﬁed scenarios are validated in PSS/E
time domain simulations.
5.4.1
Selecting the Assessment Search Width
To perform an assessment for a search depth k > 1 (e.g. N-2) it is necessary to specify a
search width. The search width was described in Chapter 3. In this chapter, it was stated
that the choice of search width is a trade-oﬀbetween the number of identiﬁed scenarios,
and the assessment computation time. This will now be investigated.
In general, for a larger search width, more line trip scenarios are investigated, and more
cascading outage scenarios will therefore most likely be identiﬁed. However, an increased
level of assessment search width does come at the cost of longer simulation time. The
computation time as a function of the search width is shown in Figure 5.13 for an N-1,
N-2 and N-3 assessment. Even though an N-3 assessment is not validated in this thesis,
the computation times for such an assessment have been included in Figure 5.13 to show
how rapidly the computation time increases for higher levels of search depth.
The assessments has been performed on a computer with an Intel Core i7 CPU @ 2.80 GHz
with 8 logical processors, 12 GB RAM, running on Windows 10, 64 bit. Matlab 2015b
has been used. If the early warning method was implemented by a system operator, more
powerful computers would most likely be used, which would lead to shorter computation
times. The main purpose of Figure 5.13 is therefore to show the diﬀerence in computation
time between the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method. The actual computation
time for a given search width shown in the ﬁgure is not of major importance.

==PAGE:86==
5. Test and Validation of the Early Warning Method
Figure 5.13 shows how the N-1 assessment computation time is independent of the search
width and remains constant at 1.75 s when the Newton-Raphson method is used, and 3.22 s,
when the TESCA method is used.
For all levels of search widths in both the N-2 and N-3 assessment, the TESCA method
takes longer time than the Newton-Raphson method. However, as it will be shown in the
upcoming assessment result analysis, the TESCA method calculate more detailed and
correct assessment results than the Newton-Raphson method. The choice between TESCA
and Newton-Raphson is thereby a trade-oﬀbetween assessment detail/correctness, and
computation time.
To select the N-2 assessment search width used for this thesis, the number of identiﬁed
scenarios as a function of the search width must be investigated. This is shown in Figure 5.14
for the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method. The identiﬁed N-2 scenarios shown
in this ﬁgure include scenarios where cascading outages occur which lead to stability or
instability, as well as scenarios where instability is detected following the initial N-1 and
N-2 line trip, without cascading outages occurring.

maintain the near real-time aspect of the early warning method, the computation time
should be taken into consideration.
For this thesis, it has been decided to use an N-2 assessment search width of 25. This
search width results in 40 and 37 scenarios for the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA
methods, respectively. This has been concluded to be an appropriate number of scenarios
to investigate in time domain simulations in order to perform a satisfactory validation of
the early warning method.
74
==PAGE:87==
5.4. Early Warning Method Identiﬁed Scenarios
5.4.2
Categorization of Scenarios
An N-1 and N-2 assessment have been performed, using both the Newton-Raphson and
the TESCA method, which has lead to a total of four performed assessments. All the
scenarios identiﬁed by the early warning method in these assessments have been validated
in PSS/E time domain simulations. By comparing the time domain simulation results with
the predictions regarding cascading outage sequences and ﬁnal states made by the early
warning method, it was found that there were certain patterns. To analyze the results
from the early warning method, it has therefore been decided to divide the simulations
into categories. For this purpose, the ﬂowchart shown in Figure 5.15 has been constructed.
Using this ﬂowchart it is possible to take the results from any of the performed time domain
simulations, compare these with the predictions made by the early warning method for the
concerned scenario, and determine a category from one of the 8 categories. The decision
boxes in the ﬂowchart will now be described.
Predicted state reached? asks if the ﬁnal state predicted by the early warning method has
been reached in the time domain simulation. Since the early warning method is capable of
using either the Newton-Raphson or the TESCA method for static security assessment,
the evaluation of these will be described individually:
• Newton-Raphson.
The outcome using this method can only be one of two:
convergence, or no convergence (divergence). A scenario where the Newton-Raphson
method is able to converge will most likely be stable. In the opposite case, a scenario
where the Newton-Raphson method is unable to converge will most likely be unstable.
According to [7, p. 216], the divergence of the Newton-Raphson method for a given
scenario can be used as an indication of instability. Based on this reference, it will be
assumed that a scenario where the Newton-Raphson method is unable to converge is
unstable. Therefore, if a scenario is predicted by the early warning method using
the Newton-Raphson method to be unable to converge and the scenario is unstable
in the time domain simulations, "Predicted state reached?" will equal "Yes". If the
time domain simulations result in a stable system, the "Predicted state reached?"
will equal "No".
• TESCA. The outcome of this method is more nuanced than for the Newton-Raphson
method. As it was shown in the screenshot of the early warning method’s Matlab
GUI (Figure 3.4), a calculation using the TESCA method can result in one of four
states: "Stable", "Aperiodic unstable", "Voltage unstable", or "Case not converged".
As described in Section 2.4.5, aperiodic instability is the loss of synchronism which
may be at just a single or several generators in the system. In the upcoming results,
there are cases where the TESCA method predicts aperiodic instability, and where
this occurs in the time domain simulations, but only for a single generator that
does not lead to overall system instability. In such a case, the TESCA method has
predicted correctly, and the "Predicted state reached?" will be answered "Yes".
Cases predicted to be "Voltage unstable" are predicted correctly if the system becomes
unstable. Voltage instability is typically connected to loss of synchronism of generators
in the system. It may be diﬃcult to diﬀerentiate between loss of synchronism and
voltage instability, and such a diﬀerentiation will therefore not be performed.
For "Case not converged" scenarios, these are predicted correctly if the system is

Figure 5.15: Flowchart where the predictions made by the early warning method are compared with
the time domain results, which leads to a category for each of the identiﬁed scenarios. Category 1
represents scenarios where the early warning method’s prediction regarding both the ﬁnal state of the
system and the cascading outage sequence leading to this state are correct. This box has therefore
been colored green. "CO" is an abbreviation for "cascading outage".

==PAGE:89==
5.4. Early Warning Method Identiﬁed Scenarios
unstable in the time domain simulations. This is using the same argument as for the
Newton-Raphson method, reference [7, p. 216].
"Predicted CO sequence completed?" asks if the exact cascading outage sequence predicted
by the early warning method has occurred in the time domain simulation. For a scenario
where no cascading outages are predicted, and none occurs, the "Predicted CO sequence
completed?" equals "Yes".
"Part of predicted CO sequence completed?" applies for cascading outage sequences,
beginning from the ﬁrst predicted cascading outage in a sequence.
This means that
the ﬁrst predicted cascading outage in a given cascading outage sequence must have
occurred for this decision box to be answered "Yes". If the answer to this decision box is
"No", then the time domain simulation has stopped at the ﬁrst line in the cascading outage
sequence, which is heavily loaded.
"Stopped at heavily loaded lines?" in this decision box, a heavily loaded line is deﬁned as
being loaded more than 85 % of its rated power.
"Single generator isolated?" applies for scenarios where a single generator is isolated
following a given cascading outage sequence. Using the TESCA method, this is detected
as "Aperiodic instability". It is not necessarily detected by the Newton-Raphson method,
if the isolation of the generator does not lead to system instability.
A scenario where the early warning method predicts the state which is reached in the
time domain simulation through the predicted cascading outage sequence is classiﬁed as
"Category 1". In this category, the early warning method’s prediction exactly matches
the result of the time domain simulations. To emphasize this match in the ﬂowchart, the
arrows leading to and the box itself for this category have been colored green.
5.4.3
Assessment Result Tables
The result of the N-1 assessment using both the Newton-Raphson and TESCA method
is shown in Table 5.3. The N-2 assessments are shown in two tables, Table 5.4 for the
Newton-Raphson method, and Table 5.5 for the TESCA method.
The columns in the tables are to be read as follows:
• The "ID" column refers to the ID of each scenario which is used as identiﬁcation in
the following analysis. The ID’s for the N-1 assessment are labeled 1.x for both the
Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method identiﬁed scenarios.
For the N-2 assessment, the scenarios identiﬁed using the Newton-Raphson method
are labeled N2.x, while the scenarios identiﬁed using the TESCA method are labeled
T2.x. There are cases where the two methods identify scenarios with the same N-1
and N-2 faults. In these scenarios, both the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA ID
are given in this column. This is done to clearly show which scenarios are identiﬁed
by both methods, and which are not. The fact that the two methods have identiﬁed
the same scenario to be investigated does not necessarily mean that they predict the
same cascading outage sequence and ﬁnal state.
77
==PAGE:90==
5. Test and Validation of the Early Warning Method
• The "N-1 Fault" and "N-2 Fault" columns show the lines which are tripped in
each scenario, determined by the betweenness centrality calculation performed by
the early warning method.
• The "Cascading Outages TESCA" and "Cascading Outages NR" columns
show the lines which are predicted by the early warning method to trip following the
N-k fault(s), due to overload. A cell in this column may contain "-" if no cascading
outages occur. It may also contain "Not id." if the scenario is only identiﬁed by either
the TESCA or the Newton-Raphson method.
• The "Cascade PSS/E" column shows the lines which trip in the performed time
domain simulation as a consequence of the N-k fault(s).
• The "Final State TESCA" column shows the reason for the termination of the
early warning method’s calculation. This may be because the system is assessed to
be stable, or that instability (aperiodic or voltage) has occurred. It may also be that
the calculation was simply not able to converge. This column may also contain "Not
id." if the scenario was only identiﬁed by the Newton-Raphson method.
• The "Final State NR" column shows the reason for the termination of the early
warning method’s calculation. Using the Newton-Raphson method, this cause may
either be a case where no lines are overloaded and the system is stable, or a case
where the Newton-Raphson method is unable to converge. This column may also
contain "Not id." if the scenario was only identiﬁed by the TESCA method.
• The "Final State PSS/E" column shows the ﬁnal state of the system determined
through the time domain simulations. Overall, this state may be either stable,
unstable, or partial blackout. In the case of an unstable system, the type of instability
will be given. In the case of a stable system, the loading of any heavily loaded lines
predicted to trip will be given.
A partial blackout is deﬁned as a scenario where loads are isolated and hence without
power supply, but where the remaining part of the system remains stable.
• The "Category NR" and Category TESCA column contains the category of each
scenario, which has been determined from the ﬂowchart in Figure 5.15.
In the N.1 assessment, Table 5.3, it is seen that there are 10 scenarios where loss of
synchronism occurs following the N-1 disturbance, and two scenarios where lines are loaded
above 99 % following this disturbance. In reality, the Swedish system would most likely be
designed and operated so that it is N-1 secure. The reason for the deviations shown in
Table 5.3 may be caused by one or more of several factors within the model. Examples of
these are: the aggregation of the many hydro power plants, the settings of the dynamic
generator models and the auxiliary equipment (AVR, governor, PSS), the setting of the line
overcurrent relays, and the location and type of reactive power compensation equipment.

5. Test and Validation of the Early Warning Method
5.5
Result Analysis
The following section will conclude on the accordance between the predictions made by
the early warning method and the results from the performed time domain simulations.
5.5.1
Evaluation of the Known N-1 Scenario (Line 51-75)
In Table 5.3 it is seen that the previously known N-1 scenario (Section 5.3.1) which was the
trip of line 51-75 has been identiﬁed by both the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method,
as scenario 1.11. However, since the early warning method performs a full N-1 assessment
of all lines in the system, it was expected that this scenario would be identiﬁed. Both the
Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method correctly predicted the ﬁrst cascading outage of
line 51-76. In the time domain simulations, it was, however, found that line 77-17077 also
trips - this was unpredicted by both methods. Following the cascading outage of line 51-76,
the TESCA method predicts that voltage instability has occurred, and the Newton-Raphson
method is unable to converge. The reason for these results may be that the voltage at
six buses in the system experiences a signiﬁcant drop to approximately 0.68 pu- 0.75 pu,
following the trip of line 51-76. For such a large voltage drop, the system may be very close
to the stability limit in the time domain simulations, which is why the TESCA method
detected voltage instability, and the Newton-Raphson method was unable to converge.
The voltages at these buses are shown in Figure 5.16.

The conclusion on this scenario is that neither the Newton-Raphson nor the TESCA
method could detect the second cascading outage of line 77-17077, which in fact lead to
a partial blackout with six buses isolated from the rest of the system. Both methods
were, however, able to detect the ﬁrst cascading outage, and both methods predicted
that instability would occur following this line trip. Although the entire cascading outage
sequence was not predicted correctly by either of the two methods, they still provided
valuable information for a system operator.
82
==PAGE:95==
5.5. Result Analysis
5.5.2
Evaluation of the Known N-2 Scenario (Line 36-39 and 34-36)
By inspecting Table 5.4 and Table 5.5, it is seen that the early warning method does
not identify the known N-2 scenario which was described in Section 5.3.2. The reason
for this lies in the betweenness centrality, and hence the physical location of the N-1
and N-2 fault lines. To investigate this, a table of showing the order of betweenness
centrality for the 150 most central branches in Sweden has been constructed, which can
be seen in Appendix A. In this appendix, it is found that line 36-39, which is the N-1
fault, has the 75th highest degree of betweenness centrality in the system. It is also seen
that line 34-36 which is the N-2 fault in this scenario, has the 119th highest degree of
betweenness centrality. The beweenness centrality of these lines is thereby not high enough
for them to be investigated in an N-2 assessment with a search width of 25. If the early
warning method was to investigate scenarios where the N-1 fault is line 36-39, it would
require a search width of 75. Following the trip of this line, the betweenness centrality of
the remaining lines in the system, including line 34-36, would change, since the system
topology has changed. If this change would increase or decrease the centrality of line 34-36
is not known. It is, however, very likely that a search width larger than 75 must be used
to investigate the N-2 fault of line 34-36 and hence identify the known N-2 scenario.
This scenario illustrates an important point regarding the workings of the early warning
method. That is, due to the betweenness centrality approach for determining the lines
for investigation, there will most likely always be scenarios where cascading outages could
occur that remain unidentiﬁed. The number of such scenarios is reduced by increasing the
search width, as was shown in Figure 5.14. However, this comes at the cost of increased
computation times, which was shown in Figure 5.13. Continuously increasing the search
width leads to longer simulation times, and the real-time aspect of the early warning
method would eventually be reduced signiﬁcantly or lost completely.
5.5.3
Predicted N-1 Scenarios
To gain an overview of the results from the N-1 assessment a bar graph has been created,
which is shown in Figure 5.17. The bars in this graph represent the number of scenarios in
each category, determined by the ﬂowchart in Figure 5.15. The scenarios in each bar are
given in percent of the total number of simulation scenarios investigated for each of the
two methods. In Table 5.3 it was seen that the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method
identify a diﬀerent number of scenarios. More speciﬁcally, the Newton-Raphson method
identiﬁes 24 scenarios, and the TESCA method identiﬁes 18 scenarios. The "Number of
scenarios [%]" on the Y-axis in the bar graph is thereby relative to this number of identiﬁed
scenarios for each of the two methods.

Figure 5.17: Bar graph showing the percentage-wise number of scenarios in each of the categories,

N-1 assessment.
From the bar graph in Figure 5.17 the following can be concluded:
1. Exact match between the early warning method’s prediction and time
domain simulation
With Category 1 being an exact match between the prediction by the early warning
method, and the result from the time domain simulation the two methods have
approximately the same correctness, with TESCA being slightly better.
2. Correct ﬁnal state predictions
Categories 1-5 include the scenarios where the ﬁnal state is predicted correctly. It is
seen that all the TESCA method’s predictions are within these ﬁve categories. This
is not the case for Newton-Raphson, which has a total of 16.7 % in categories outside
of this interval.
3. Simulation stop at heavily loaded lines
The Newton-Raphson method has 8.3 % of the investigated scenarios in Category 8.
In this category, the time domain simulation has stopped due to a heavily loaded
line. The TESCA method does not identify these scenarios.
4. The TESCA method identiﬁes aperiodic instability for a single generator
In scenarios 1.22, 1.23, 1.24, and 1.25 it is seen that the TESCA method determines
aperiodic instability after only a single cascading outage. In all four cases, the Newton-
Raphson method calculates at least one more cascading outage. This diﬀerence
between the two methods in these four scenarios is due to the fact that tripping of
lines 115-120 and 119-120 or 119-126 and 126-10129 causes a generator to become
isolated. The two generators at these buses are producing 600 MW and 800 MW for
bus 120 and bus 126, respectively. The TESCA method thereby correctly predicts
that aperiodic instability has occurred following a single cascading outage, but only
for a single generator. This is, however, not enough to cause overall system instability.
In fact, it is seen in scenario 1.22 and 1.23 that both generator 120 and 126 are
isolated in the time domain simulations before instability occurs. From this, it is
concluded, that the TESCA method investigates if aperiodic instability has ocurred
for each generator, while the Newton-Raphson method looks at the overall stability
84
==PAGE:97==
5.5. Result Analysis
of the system. This may be seen as either an advantage or disadvantage for each
method, depending on which information is desired about the system.
5. The TESCA method identiﬁes aperiodically unstable scenarios which the
Newton-Raphson method does not
The Newton-Raphson method identiﬁes the same scenarios as the TESCA method,
with two exceptions, scenarios 1.3 and 1.6. By investigation of the system, it is found
that the trip of either line 16-11216 (scenario 1.3) or line 23-12123 (scenario 1.6)
causes a smaller generator to become isolated. This is therefore detected as aperiodic
instability by the TESCA method. Due to the modest size of the generator, it
is, however, not detected by the Newton-Raphson method, as it does not cause
instability in the system. This observation corresponds with what was concluded in
the previous point.
6. The Newton-Raphson method identiﬁes partial blackout scenarios which
the TESCA method does not
The scenarios 1.7, 1.14 and 1.21 are only identiﬁed by the Newton-Raphson method.
In these scenarios, partial blackouts occur, which is where an amount of load is
isolated, and hence without power supply. The fact that these scenarios are not
identiﬁed is a serious ﬂaw in the TESCA method because it provides a false security
for the system operator, who is unaware that a single line trip may cause a partial
blackout.
Based on the points stated above, it is concluded that the TESCA method performs
better than the Newton-Raphson method in the N-1 assessment. TESCA predicts all
the ﬁnal states correctly, whereas the Newton-Raphson method has deviations from this.
The TESCA method does, however, have drawbacks, since it is not able to identify three
scenarios where partial blackouts occur.

constructed for the N-2 assessment. This is shown in Figure 5.18.

Figure 5.18: Bar graph showing the percentage-wise number of scenarios in each of the categories,
