From the bar graph in Figure 5.18 the following conclusions can be made:
1. Exact match between the early warning method’s prediction and time
domain simulation
In the N-1 assessment, the TESCA and the Newton-Raphson method had 61.1 %
and 50 % of the predicted scenarios in Category 1, which is an exact match between
prediction and time domain simulation. In the N-2 assessment, this is now reduced
signiﬁcantly to 35.1 % for TESCA, and 22.5 % for Newton-Raphson. The TESCA
method still makes the largest number of correct predictions regarding ﬁnal states
and cascading outage sequences.
2. Correct ﬁnal state predictions
In the N-1 assessment, the TESCA method has all its predictions within categories
1-5, and thereby predict all the examined ﬁnal states correctly. This now only applies
to 59.4 % of the scenarios. For Newton-Raphson this is even lower, at 37.5 %.
3. Prediction where no cascading outages are predicted and none occurs
Category 6 contains scenarios where there is no prediction of cascading outages
following the initial line trips, and where none occurs.
It has been found, by
inspecting Table 5.3, Table 5.4 and Table 5.5, that this category contains 8 scenarios,
where 7 of these occur following the trip of line 70-71. By inspecting the model, it is
found that tripping line 70-71 causes a total load of 1207 MW to be connected to
the nearest generator through a large reactance caused by long lines. When the time
domain simulation is stable, and the TESCA and the Newton-Raphson methods
determine the scenario to be unstable, it is most likely because the buses 71 and 72
are operating close to their stability limit. In all of these scenarios, the voltage at
these buses decreases to approximately 0.91 pu, which is an indication that voltage
instability is close. This can be desribed using the PV-curve shown in Figure 2.6.
86
==PAGE:99==
5.5. Result Analysis
4. Simulation stop at heavily loaded lines
A general issue which has been identiﬁed in the N-2 assessment is that the time
domain simulation stops at heavily loaded lines which were predicted to trip by either
of the two methods. Such lines are loaded > 85 % and in several of the investigated
scenarios > 95 %. The simulation stop due to a heavily loaded line is the case in
the Categories 2, 3, 7 and 8. Adding the scenarios in these categories together
equals 37.8 % of the total number of scenarios for the TESCA method, and 57.5 %
for the Newton-Raphson method.
When the early warning method uses the TESCA method for static security
assessment, it has been given the dynamic generator models. The early warning
method should therefore be able to correctly calculate the new line power ﬂow
following a line trip, since it uses the droop-characteristics of the governors in
the system. When deviations occur, which leads to line loadings being incorrectly
predicted, it may be caused by the diﬀerence in load modeling between the TESCA
method and the time domain simulation. The TESCA method models loads as
constant power, whereas the time domain simulation used the previously described
ZIP model where only 25 % of the active power is modeled as constant power, and
where the reactive power is represented entirely as constant impedance.
The Newton-Raphson method is, opposite to the TESCA method, unaware of the
droop-characteristic of the governors in the system. This method only has the power
production from the slack bus generator to adjust and thereby balance the power
ﬂow in the system following a line trip. The time domain simulation uses all the
generators participating in the frequency control. Therefore, the power ﬂow following
a line trip will inevitably be diﬀerent in the time domain simulations, compared with
the power ﬂow calculated by the Newton-Raphson method. For the N-2 scenarios,
the slack bus has changed its production twice and is therefore more likely to diﬀer
signiﬁcantly from the time domain simulation, compared to the N-1 assessment.
Based on these points, it is concluded that the TESCA method once again produces the
best predictions when comparing with time domain simulations. However, the accuracy of
both the TESCA and the Newton-Raphson method has decreased signiﬁcantly in the N-2
assessment due to the additional line trip that is applied on the system.
87
==PAGE:100==
5. Test and Validation of the Early Warning Method
5.5.5
Evaluation of the Predicted Cascading Outage Sequence for the
N-2 Assessment
The bar graph for the N-2 assessment in Figure 5.18 shows how the investigated scenarios
are divided into categories based on both the ﬁnal state and the cascading outages (if
any) leading to this state. It is, however, also interesting to only investigate the cascading
outages which lead to the ﬁnal state. There are three possibilities for this. These are where
the time domain simulations show:
• the exact same cascading outages (if any) as predicted by the early warning method.
This includes the categories 1 and 6.
• that the predicted cascading outage sequence is not completed and that the reason
for this is that the next line in the predicted cascading outage sequence is heavily
loaded. This includes the categories 2, 3, 7 and 8.
• that an unpredicted or reverse order cascading outage has occurred. This includes
categories 4 and 5.
To get an overview of how the scenarios in these categories are distributed for the TESCA
and the Newton-Raphson method, the pie charts shown in Figure 5.19 have been created.

5.5. Result Analysis
The pie charts in Figure 5.19 clearly show what was also concluded in Section 5.5.4. That
is, for both the TESCA and the Newton-Raphson method there is an issue of heavily
loaded lines not tripping, and unpredicted lines which trip. It is seen in the pie charts that
this issue is worst when using the Newton-Raphson method. The reason for this will be
investigated in the following section.
5.5.6
Time Domain Simulation With Disabled Governors
In the evaluation of the results from the N-2 assessment, Section 5.5.4, it was found that
several of the investigated scenarios contained lines which were predicted to trip, but
were in fact only heavily loaded in the time domain simulation. It was assumed that the
reason for this when using the Newton-Raphson method was that this method only has
the slack bus generator to balance the power ﬂow in the system. This deviates from the
time domain simulations and will therefore most likely cause incorrect predictions. To
investigate if this assumption is in fact valid, a time domain simulation has been executed
- with all governors disabled, except the one at the slack bus generator. This will make the
time domain simulation work in a similar manner as the calculation the Newton-Raphson
method performs. Therefore, it should be possible to obtain approximately similar results
in this time domain simulation as the Newton-Raphson method calculates in the early
warning method.
To perform this investigation, scenario N2.4 from Table 5.4 has been chosen. The reason for
choosing this scenario is that the sequence of cascading outages which is predicted by the
Newton-Raphson method is not completed in the time domain simulations. Instead, it is
stopped at a heavily loaded line, namely line 73-130, which is loaded 96.3 %. Therefore, it
should be possible to make line 73-130 trip in a time domain simulation where all governors
in the system are disabled, excluding the slack bus generator. For convenience, the lines of
interest in this scenario are rewritten in Table 5.6.
Table 5.6: The scenario from the N-2 assessment using the Newton-Raphson method, which is
simulated in the time domain with disabled governors, excluding the slack bus generator.

73-130 96.3 % loaded
The time domain simulation has been executed under the following conditions which diﬀer
from what was speciﬁed in Section 5.1:
• The "Network frequency dependence" is disabled. This feature is not included in the
Newton-Raphson method and must therefore be disabled to achieve similar results.
• In the previously performed time domain simulations, the ZIP load modeling created
active power loads containing parts of constant Z, I and P. The reactive power loads
were only represented by constant Z. However, the Newton-Raphson method models
loads as being constant power, and the ZIP model must therefore be changed. It was
not possible to perform a time domain simulation using constant power for active and
reactive loads. Instead, a load reset model was used (the PSS/E model EXTLAL).
This is assessed to be a fair approximation of constant power loads.

• N-2 line trip is applied at t = 1000 s.
• All governors except the one at the slack bus generator are disabled.

the simulation - the slack bus generator. It is therefore concluded that the governors on
the remaining generators are disabled as intended.

5. Test and Validation of the Early Warning Method
The results shown in Figure 5.21 will now be evaluated for the three cases.
• Line loading, Figure 5.21a: This ﬁgure shows the loading on the N-1 and N-2
fault lines as well as the two lines which are predicted by the early warning method
to trip.
By inspecting this ﬁgure, it is seen that there is a good match between the line
loading on all four lines in the three simulation cases until the N-2 trip occurs. After
this line trip, the power ﬂow in the lines 32-12832 and 73-130 begins to deviate in the
three cases. This deviation leads to a diﬀerence in the trip time for the ﬁrst cascading
outage of line 32-12832 of approximately 200 seconds. After the cascading outage of
line 32-12832, which occurs in all three cases, it is seen that the remaining line 73-130
is not overloaded in the time domain simulation where all governors are in service.
This is expected as this was the result previously obtained using this simulation
model/setting, shown in Table 5.6. It is also seen how both the Newton-Raphson
load ﬂow and the disabled governor time domain simulation calculate the line to
be overloaded. It was expected that the loading on line 73-130 was approximately
identical in these two cases. However, there is a minor deviation.
• Slack bus generator active power production, Figure 5.21b: In this ﬁgure it
is seen how the slack bus generator increases its power production following each line
trip in the Newton-Raphson load ﬂow calculation. This is expected as this generator
is the only one to provide power to supply the additional losses which follow as a
consequence of the tripped lines.
When comparing the power production in the two time domain simulations, there
is a clear diﬀerence. While the power production in the enabled governor scenario
changes very little during the simulation, the production in the disabled governor
scenario increases more. However, it was expected that the power production in the
Newton-Raphson load ﬂow and the time domain disabled governor scenario were
closer to each other.
• Frequency, Figure 5.21c: In the Newton-Raphson load ﬂow, the frequency is
constant at 50 Hz. The frequency is not a variable in this calculation method, so it
cannot change. When comparing the two time domain simulations, it is clear that
there is a signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the frequency.
In the enabled governor case, it is seen that there is a slight overfrequency. This
is caused by the fact that as the lines in the system trips, the voltage generally
decreases throughout the system. This causes the loads to consume less power, which
creates an excess of active power in the system, and hence an overfrequency.
Before the second cascading outage in the disabled governor case, which causes system
instability, the frequency is at 49.75 Hz. If the slack bus generator was producing
more power than shown in Figure 5.21b, this frequency would have been increased.
It would therefore most likely be possible to obtain a better match between slack bus
generator power production in the Newton-Raphson load ﬂow and the time domain
simulation than there is now. If secondary control had been implemented on the slack
bus generator’s governor, the frequency would have returned to nominal (assuming
that the maximum power from the generator is not exceeded), caused by additional
power production from this generator.
92
==PAGE:105==
5.5. Result Analysis
The conclusion on this is that it has not been possible to perform a time domain simulation
which provided results that exactly resembled the Newton-Raphson load ﬂow. It is, however,
believed that this is due to the fact that the slack bus generator does not produce enough
power to maintain nominal frequency. Had this been the case, it is assumed that there
would be a better match between the two. To validate this assumption would require
additional control system implementation on the slack bus governor, and this has therefore
not been investigated further.
93
==PAGE:106==
Conclusion 6
6.1
Discussion of Results
The purpose of this thesis was to validate the newly developed "The early warning
method". The early warning method has previously only been validated through time
domain simulations on the medium size Nordic32 test system, which contains 52 lines.
It was therefore of interest to validate the method on a large-scale system, which has
been performed in this thesis. For this purpose, a detailed PSS/E model of the Swedish
transmission system with 182 lines was used.
The early warning method oﬀers the possibility of using either the well-known Newton-
Raphson or the newly developed TESCA method to perform static security assessments
following branch disconnections. The performance of both methods has been investigated
in the thesis where N-1 and N-2 assessments have been performed. This led to a total
of 87 identiﬁed scenarios which were validated by time domain simulations. Through these
simulations, several observations were made which will now be described.
The early warning method determines which branches to disconnect based on a betweenness
centrality calculation of the branches in the system. When using such an approach, it
is only the system topology which is taken into account. The loading of each branch is
thereby not considered. Neglecting the branch loadings and only investigating the most
central branches can lead to scenarios where branches with a lower degree of centrality that
can initiate cascading outages are being overlooked. Such an N-2 scenario was manually
identiﬁed where it was found that the reason for this line not being investigated by the
early warning method was its low centrality.
In the N-1 assessment, it was found that the early warning method was very reliable.
When the TESCA method was used, all the predictions of ﬁnal states were correct. The
Newton-Raphson method did not perform quite as well as the TESCA method and made
a few incorrect ﬁnal state predictions. In the N-1 assessment, it was also found that the
TESCA method made more correct predictions on cascading outage sequences than the
Newton-Raphson method. However, there were a few scenarios where partial blackouts
occurred which the TESCA method did not detect. This is believed to be a serious ﬂaw in
the TESCA method, as it provides a false security for the system operator, who is unaware
that a single N-1 fault may cause a partial blackout.
In the N-2 assessment, it was found that both the TESCA and the Newton-Raphson
method made fewer correct predictions compared to the N-1 assessment. In many of the
investigated cases, both methods predicted line trips which did not occur in the time domain
simulations. However, several of the lines predicted to trip were heavily loaded, above 95 %.
94
==PAGE:107==
6.1. Discussion of Results
The TESCA method was still the most accurate in the N-2 assessment regarding the
number of correct ﬁnal state predictions. The issue where lines were predicted to trip, but
were in fact only heavily loaded is a very relevant observation of a problem which occurs
for assessment depths larger than N-1.
One reason for the wrongful prediction of line trips in the N-2 assessment may be the load
modeling in the time domain simulations, versus the load modeling in the Newton-Raphson
and the TESCA method. A ZIP load model was used in the time domain model whereas
both Newton-Raphson and TESCA modeled loads as being constant power. In the case
of a voltage deviation from nominal, the load in the time domain simulation would diﬀer
from the predictions made by both the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method. This
would cause an unpredicted power ﬂow.
When analyzing the N-2 assessment results, it was found that the Newton-Raphson method
predicted more line trips which were only heavily loaded than the TESCA method did.
It was assumed that the reason for the Newton-Raphson method to lose accuracy over
TESCA was due to the way this method balances the power ﬂow in the system. The
Newton-Raphson method only has the slack bus generator to do this, while the time domain
simulation as well as TESCA use all generators participating in the system’s frequency
control. It was attempted to validate this hypothesis through a time domain simulation
where all governors in the system except the one at the slack bus generator were disabled.
It was found that this caused the system to behave in a way close to the Newton-Raphson
method. The hypothesis that the Newton-Raphson method causes deviations from the
time domain simulations due to the way it balances the power ﬂow was thereby conﬁrmed.
An interesting observation was made when a single generator became isolated. In such a
case, the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA methods predicted diﬀerently, and each method
had its own advantage. When a generator became isolated, TESCA would detect aperiodic
instability, which is correct - for that single generator. Newton-Raphson would, however,
only be unable to converge if the isolation of the generator was critical enough to cause
a system blackout. It can therefore be concluded for such a case that TESCA analyzes
the stability of the individual generator, while the Newton-Raphson method analyzes the
stability of the overall system. The information provided by each method in such a case
may be valuable for a system operator.
The early warning method’s computation time using the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA
method has been investigated. This has been done for an N-1, N-2 and N-3 assessment,
with varying search widths. Here it was found that the TESCA method takes longer time
than the Newton-Raphson method for a given search width. The identiﬁed scenarios for
the two methods were also investigated, and it was found that both methods identiﬁed
approximately the same number of scenarios for a given search width. With TESCA
providing more correct predictions, the additional computation time may be well spent.
The overall conclusion of the early warning method is that it is quite accurate for an
N-1 assessment where the use of the TESCA method is preferred - not considering the
computation time aspect. For the N-2 assessment, the early warning method becomes less
reliable. This applies for both the Newton-Raphson and the TESCA method. The TESCA
method does, however, still make the largest number of correct predictions.
95
==PAGE:108==
6. Conclusion
6.2
Future Work
Implementation of Branch Loading
The fact that branch loading is not considered when identifying which initiating faults to
investigate may lead to scenarios which are unidentiﬁed due to highly loaded branches
with a low degree of betweenness centrality. The early warning method could therefore be
extended to take this into the assessment regarding which initiating faults to investigate.
Implementation of Additional Component Protection
The early warning method only investigates cascading outages of lines, and if overexcitation
limiters has activated (only using the TESCA method). The method could be extended
to include protection of other components such as transformers and generators. More
advanced line protection such as distance relays could also be implemented.
Implementation of Additional Initiating Faults
Currently, the early warning method only uses line or transformer disconnections as
initiating faults. However, cascading outages can in reality be initiated by other types of
faults, e.g. the trip of a generator. The early warning method could therefore be extended
to include additional types of initiating faults.
The TESCA method Should Inform on Single Generator Aperiodic Instability
In cases where a single generator becomes isolated, TESCA stops the computation, and
determines that aperiodic instability has occurred. The power rating of the generator is
not considered, which may be small and insigniﬁcant for the overall stability of the system.
The early warning method’s TESCA implementation could therefore be extended so that
it continues the computation following the isolation of a single generator and displays this
information in the Matlab GUI.
The TESCA method Should Detect Partial Blackout Scenarios
It was found that the TESCA method did not identify a few partial blackout scenarios
which the Newton-Raphson method did. This should be changed, so that the false security
this causes is eliminated.
It would also be convenient to state the size and thereby
severity of the isolated load in the Matlab GUI. In the investigated cases, this ranges
from 50 MW- 1200 MW.
Implementation of More Advanced Load Modeling
It was found that the diﬀerent ways of modeling load in the TESCA/Newton-Raphson
method compared to the time domain simulations lead to incorrect line trip predictions.
In reality, loads are far more complex than the simple constant power representations used
in both the TESCA and the Newton-Raphson method. The implementation of a more
advanced load model could therefore be useful in terms of improving the accuracy of the
early warning method.
96
==PAGE:109==
6.3. Evaluation of Learning Objectives
6.3
Evaluation of Learning Objectives
The learning objectives which were deﬁned in Section 1.3 will now be evaluated.
1. Identify the PSS/E model requirements for a dynamic stability study of this type.
• Section 4.2, Dynamic Model Requirements, described the purpose and need for
various dynamic generator models. In this section it was also determined that
it was necessary to implement line overcurrent relays to investigate cascading
outages of lines.
2. Gain an understanding of overcurrent relays and the modeling of these.
• Section 2.5, Maximum Line Loading, described the need for overcurrent pro-
tection relays on overhead transmission lines. In Section 4.2, Dynamic Model
Requirements, it was determined that it is necessary to include such overcurrent
relays when performing a dynamic cascading outage study of transmission
lines. Then, in Section 4.3.1, Addition of Line Overcurrent Protection Relays,
TIOCR1, the workings of the applied PSS/E TIOCR1 model was described.
It was determined that to apply this model, a maximum line current must be
calculated, and that this calculation was dependent on the maximum allowable
conductor temperature, the wind speed at the conductor, and the ambient air
temperature. The inﬂuence of each parameter was investigated, and appropriate
values were chosen. The implementation of the TIOCR1 model in PSS/E was
hereafter described and a Matlab function capable of creating the TIOCR1
models in a txt ﬁle was developed.
3. Modify and extend an already existing PSS/E simulation model to this project.
• Section 4.3, Model Modiﬁcation and Extension, described how the original model
has undergone a thorough investigation. Eight points were identiﬁed, and the
modiﬁcation/extension of these were described. This part of the thesis proved
to be quite extensive due to the size and complexity of the model.
4. Apply a state of the art research method for analyzing cascading outages in electric
power systems.
• Following the modiﬁcation and extension of the model, the early warning method
was successfully applied for an N-1 and N-2 assessment, using both methods of
static security assessment. The inﬂuence of the search width on the computation
time and the number of identiﬁed scenarios has been investigated. The results
of this were given in Section 5.4, Early Warning Method Identiﬁed Scenarios.
5. Apply PSS/E to perform an extensive power system simulation study.
• Following the application of the early warning method, the identiﬁed scenarios
were validated in PSS/E. This lead to a total of 87 dynamic simulations. To
handle the extensive output data from each simulation it was deemed necessary
to construct two Matlab functions, loadScenario and plotScenario.
These functions were described in Section 5.2, Stability Assessment.
97
==PAGE:110==
6. Conclusion
6. Analyze and simulate the mechanisms causing blackouts through cascading outages.
• Throughout this thesis it became evident that the study of cascading outages
is a complex topic where many ﬁelds and mechanisms within power system
stability are included. Some of these were described in Chapter 2, Theory. These
mechanisms were also analyzed in relation to the simulation model, in Section 5.3,
Known Cascading Outage Scenarios, where an N-1 and N-2 cascading outage
scenario were manually identiﬁed. The events from the initiating line trips, until
instability occurred, was analyzed in detail for these scenarios.
Based on this, it is concluded that all learning objectives have been completely fulﬁlled.
98

1 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
2 % This script constains the calculation of the maximum current and power
%
3 % for each of the two line types. A sweep of the parameters used for this %
4 % calculation is also shown included.
%
5 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
6 clear; clc; close all;

8 set(0,’defaultaxesfontname’,’times’)

14 %% Calculate the maximum current and power transfer in each line type
15 clc;
16 T_a = 30; T_s = 95; V_w = 0.6; Sbase = 1000e6;
17 %
var2txt(T_a,0,’T_a.tex’,’\\degreeCelsius’);
18 %
var2txt(T_s,0,’T_s.tex’,’\\degreeCelsius’);
19 %
var2txt(V_w,1,’V_w.tex’,’m/s’);
20
21 LineR = [0.048;0.034]; % 0.048 ohm/km for 220 kV and 0.034 ohm/km for 400 kV.
22 Vbus
= [220;400];
23 LineLengths = [1;1]; % 1 km of each line type
24

26 %
var2txt(Imax(1),0,’Imax220.tex’,’’);
27 %
var2txt(Imax(2),0,’Imax400.tex’,’’);
28 %
var2txt(Imax_pu(1),3,’Imax220pu.tex’,’’);
29 %
var2txt(Imax_pu(2),3,’Imax400pu.tex’,’’);
30 %
var2txt(Slimit(1),0,’Slimit220.tex’,’’);
31 %
var2txt(Slimit(2),0,’Slimit400.tex’,’’);
32

34
’VariableNames’,{’Imax_A’,’Imax_pu’,’Slimit_MVA’}, ...
35
’RowNames’,{’220 kV’,’400 kV’})

45 end

B. MATLAB
B.5
LineLoading MATLAB Function
1 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
2 %
%
3 %
Program that calculates loading limits of overhead lines
%
4 %
%
5 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
6 % The function calculates the steady state line current limit in A, and the
7 % corresponding apparent power limit at rated voltage in MVA.
8 %
9 % The calculations are based on IEEE Std 738-2012, "IEEE Standard for
10 % Calculating the Current-Temperature of Bare Overhead Conductors".
11
12 function [I_max,Imax_pu,Slimit] = LineLoading(LineR,LineLengths,Vbus, T_a, T_s, V_w,
Sbase)
13
14 % Setting constants and assumptions for the calculations
15 epsylon=0.5; % emissivity
16 Qs=1023; % Total solar and sky radiated geat flux rate in w/m2
17 alpha=0.5; % Solar absorptivity
18 theta=1.570796; % Effective angle incidence of sun’s rays
19 rho_DC=2.83e-8; % DC resistivity of aluminium @ 20 deg. C in ohm m
20
21 rho_f=1; %Density of air in kg/m3. @40 deg. and 1000m it is 1 kg/m3
22 %T_c=100; %Maximum conductor temperature in deg. C.
23 %T_a=25; %Ambient temperature in deg. C
24 %V_w=1; % Wind speed perpendiculat to the line in m/s
25 theta=90; % Effective angle of incidence of the sun’s rays in deg.
26 phi=90; % Angle between wind and axis of conductor in deg.
27 mu_f=2.04e-5; % Dynamic viscosity of air in Pa*s
28
29 %% Diameter of conductor
30 % Crosssectional area of conductor
31 Am2=rho_DC.*LineLengths*1000./LineR; % Area in m2
32
33 %Diameter
34 D=2*sqrt(Am2./pi); % Diameter in m
35 %Dmm=D*1000 % Diameter in mm
36
37 %% Convective heat loss
38 % Reynolds Number
39 N_Re=(D.*rho_f*V_w)./mu_f;
40
41 %% Natural Convection
42 q_cn=3.645*rho_f^0.5.*D.^0.75*(T_s-T_a)^1.25;
43
44 %% Forced Convection
45 K_angle=1.194-cosd(phi)+0.194*cosd(2*phi)+0.368*sind(2*phi);
46
47 T_film=(T_s+T_a)/2;
48 k_f=2.424e-2+7.477e-5*T_film-4.407e-9*T_film^2;
49
50 q_c1=K_angle*(1.01+1.35*N_Re.^0.52)*k_f*(T_s-T_a); % Convection at low wind
51
52 q_c2=K_angle*0.754*N_Re.^(0.6)*k_f*(T_s-T_a); % Convection at High wind
53
54 if mean(q_c1)>mean(q_c2)
55
q_fc=q_c1;
56 %
disp(’
Low Wind’)
57 else
58
q_fc=q_c2;
59 %
disp(’
High Wind’)
60 end
61
62 %fprintf(’----------------------- \n
Wind = %d \n’,V_w);
63
112
==PAGE:125==
B.5. LineLoading MATLAB Function
64 %% forced or natural
65 if mean(q_fc)>mean(q_cn)
66
q_c=q_fc;
67 %
fprintf(’
Forced Convection\n’)
68 %
fprintf(’
q_c = %d \n’,q_c);
69 else
70
q_c=q_cn;
71 %
fprintf(’
Natural Convection\n’)
72 %
fprintf(’
q_c = %d \n’,q_c);
73 end
74
75 % Radiated Heat Loss
76 q_r=17.8*D*epsylon*(((T_s+273)/100)^4-((T_a+273)/100)^4);
77
78 % Solar Heat Gain
79 % Projected area of conductor pr unit length, A’ [m2/m]
80
81 Amark=D; % The sun is shining perpendicular to the line
82 q_s=alpha*Qs*sind(theta).*Amark;
83
84 %% Steady State Heat Balance
85 R_tc=LineR./LineLengths/1000;
86
87 I_max=sqrt((q_c+q_r-q_s)./R_tc); % Current limit in A
88
89 %MVA limit in MVA
90 Slimit=I_max.*(Vbus*1e3)*sqrt(3)/1e6;
91
92 Ibase = Sbase./(sqrt(3)*Vbus*10^3);
93 Imax_pu = I_max./Ibase;
94
95 %fprintf(’
Imax = %d
\n’,I_max);
96
97 return
113
==PAGE:126==
B. MATLAB
B.6
loadScenario MATLAB Function
1 function [time,data,names] = loadScenario(N1_from,N1_to,N2_from,N2_to)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % This function loads the converted data from a PSS/E .out-file.
%
4 % This is done by specifying the lines which has been trippped as initial %
5 % disturbance as input. The function removes any negative timesteps from
%
6 % the converted .out-file.
%
7 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
8
9 if isempty(N2_from) || isempty(N2_to)
10
filename = strcat(’C:/Users/Christian/Desktop/Input_N1_N2/N1/’,num2str(N1_from),’_’,
num2str(N1_to),’m.mat’);
11 else
12
filename = strcat(’C:/Users/Christian/Desktop/Input_N1_N2/N2/’,num2str(N1_from),’_’,
num2str(N1_to),’_’,num2str(N2_from),’_’,num2str(N2_to),’m.mat’);
13 end
14 load(filename);
15
16 % remove the columns with negative times
17 zeroReached = 0;
18 while zeroReached <= 0
19
if Data(1,1) < 0
20
Data(:,1)=[];
21

1 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
2 % This script contains plots of all the N-1 scenarios. The plots include
%
3 % the overview given by the "plotScenario" function, as well as plots of

4 % all the lines mentioned by the early warning method, for each scenario. %

6 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

1 function saveTightFigure(h,outfilename)
2 % Saves figure H in file outfilename without the white space around it.
3
4 % saveTightFigure(gfc,’TEST.pdf’)
5
6 if nargin == 0
7

15 dv.a = get(0, ’DefaultAxesVisible’);
16 set(0, ’DefaultFigureVisible’, ’on’)
17 set(0, ’DefaultAxesVisible’, ’on’)
18
19 %% copy figure
20 h_to_print = figure;
21 copyobj(get(h,’children’),h_to_print);
22
23 %% put old default visible parameters
24 set(0, ’DefaultFigureVisible’, dv.f)
25 set(0, ’DefaultAxesVisible’, dv.a)
26
27 %% find all the axes in the figure
28 hax = findall(h_to_print, ’type’, ’axes’);
29
30 %% compute the tighest box that includes all axes
31 tighest_box = [Inf Inf -Inf -Inf]; % left bottom right top
32 for i=1:length(hax)
33
set(hax(i), ’units’, ’centimeters’);
34
35
p = get(hax(i), ’position’);
36
ti = get(hax(i), ’tightinset’);
37
38
% get position as left, bottom, right, top
39
p = [p(1) p(2) p(1)+p(3) p(2)+p(4)] + ti.*[-1 -1 1 1];
40
41
tighest_box(1) = min(tighest_box(1), p(1));
42
tighest_box(2) = min(tighest_box(2), p(2));
43
tighest_box(3) = max(tighest_box(3), p(3));
44
tighest_box(4) = max(tighest_box(4), p(4));
45 end
46
47 %% move all axes to left-bottom
48 for i=1:length(hax)
49
if strcmp(get(hax(i),’tag’),’legend’)
50
continue
51
end
52
p = get(hax(i), ’position’);
53
set(hax(i), ’position’, [p(1)-tighest_box(1) p(2)-tighest_box(2) p(3) p(4)]);
54 end
55
56 %% resize figure to fit tightly
57 set(h_to_print, ’units’, ’centimeters’);
58 p = get(h_to_print, ’position’);
59
60 width = tighest_box(3)-tighest_box(1);
61 height =
tighest_box(4)-tighest_box(2);
62 set(h_to_print, ’position’, [p(1) p(2) width height]);
63
64 %% set papersize
144
==PAGE:157==
B.17. saveTightFigure MATLAB Function
65 set(h_to_print,’PaperUnits’,’centimeters’);
66 set(h_to_print,’PaperSize’, [width height]);
67 %set(h_to_print,’PaperSize’, [2.1*width height]);
68 set(h_to_print,’PaperPositionMode’, ’manual’);
69 %set(h_to_print,’PaperPosition’,[0 0 2*width height]);
70 set(h_to_print,’PaperPosition’,[0 0 width height]);
71
72 %% save it
73 saveas(h_to_print,outfilename);
74

76 close(h_to_print);

B. MATLAB
B.18
TIOCR1_settings MATLAB Function
1 function TIOCR1_settings(input,filename)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % This function creates a .txt-file which the settings of the TIOCR1
%
4 % protection models, used for the dynamic simulations. This is done by
%
5 % using a input file which contains the to-bus, from-bus, Vbus, and the
%
6 % rated current for each line. This file is an output from the early
%
7 % warning method. This ensures, that the early warning method and the
%
8 % TIOCR1 models are operating under the excact same conditions.
%
9 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
10 filename = strcat(filename,’.txt’);
11 fid = fopen(filename,’wt’);
12
13 from = input(:,1); to = input(:,2); Imax = input(:,4);
14
15 % Relay data:
16
T0
= 30.000;
17
CUR1 = 1.0700;
18
T1
= 15.000;
19
CUR2 = 1.1770;
20
T2
= 7.3200;
21
CUR3 = 1.4000;
22
T3
= 3.57;
23
CUR4 = 2.0000;
24
T4
= 1.7350;
25
CUR5 = 3.0000;
26
T5
= 1.00000;
27
TB
= 0.50000;
28
FRAC = 0.0000;
29
30 % insert zeros to allow for parallel line indexing
31 from = [0; from]; to = [0; to]; Imax = [0; Imax];
32
33 string1 = ’%d \’’TIOCR1\’’\t%d\t%d\t\t1\t\t1\n’;
34 string2 = ’\t0\t\t\’’0 \’’\t%d\t%d\t\’’%d \’’\n’;
35 string3 = ’\t0\t\t 0\t\’’0 \’’\t0\t0\n’;
36 string4 = ’\t\’’0 \’’\t%.4f\t%.3f\t%.4f %.3f \n’;
37 string5 = ’\t%.4f\t%.4f\t%.4f
%.4f %.4f \n’;
38 string6 = ’\t%.4f\t%.4f\t%.5f
%.5f %.4f /\n’;
39
40 for line = 2:length(from)
41
42
if (from(line) == from(line-1)) && (to(line)==to(line-1)) % check for parallel lines
43
id = 2;
44
else
45
id = 1;
46
end
47
48
fprintf(fid,string1,from(line),to(line),id);
49
fprintf(fid,string2,from(line),to(line),id);
50
fprintf(fid,string3);
51
fprintf(fid,string4,Imax(line),T0,CUR1,T1);
52
fprintf(fid,string5,CUR2,T2,CUR3,T3,CUR4);
53
fprintf(fid,string6,T4,CUR5,T5,TB,FRAC);
54
55
fprintf(fid,string1,to(line),from(line),id);
56
fprintf(fid,string2,from(line),to(line),id);
57
fprintf(fid,string3);
58
fprintf(fid,string4,Imax(line),T0,CUR1,T1);
59
fprintf(fid,string5,CUR2,T2,CUR3,T3,CUR4);
60
fprintf(fid,string6,T4,CUR5,T5,TB,FRAC);
61 end
62
63 fclose(fid);
146
==PAGE:159==
B.19. var2txt MATLAB Function
B.19
var2txt MATLAB Function
1 function var2txt(variable,decimals,filename,unit)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % This function takes a variable as input, and saves it as a .txt-file.
%
4 % This us used to import variables from MATLAB into LaTeX.
%
5 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
6
7 fileID = fopen(filename,’w’); % Open file with write permission.
8
9 realPart = strcat(’\\SI{%.’,num2str(decimals),’f}{’,unit,’}’);
10
11 if imag(variable) < 0
12
imagPart = strcat(’-j%.’,num2str(decimals),’f’);
13
total = strcat(realPart,imagPart);
14
fprintf(fileID,total,real(variable),abs(imag(variable))); % Write variable to file.
15 elseif imag(variable) > 0
16
imagPart = strcat(’+j%.’,num2str(decimals),’f’);
17
total = strcat(realPart,imagPart);
18
fprintf(fileID,total,real(variable),imag(variable)); % Write variable to file.
19 elseif imag(variable) == 0
20
fprintf(fileID,realPart,real(variable)); % Write variable to file.
21 end
22
23 fclose(fileID); % Close the file.
24
25 movefile(filename,’../variables/’,’f’);
147
